Divorce by Gesture with Fingers: Determining the Number
Ruling with proof from hadith and application of customary gesture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of divorces when a husband uses a finger gesture with the word 'haka', and distinguish between diyanatan and qada'an in such cases.

Core Idea
The gesture with fingers determines the number of divorces; intention contrary to apparent is accepted diyanatan but not qada'an.

This passage from the Hidayah chapter on divorce addresses a specific scenario: when a husband says 'You are divorced like this' and makes a finger gesture. The Hanafi school, relying on a hadith of the Prophet (s.a.w.), holds that the customary meaning of the gesture determines the number of divorces. However, a distinction is drawn between what is accepted religiously (diyanatan) and what is enforced judicially (qada'an). Understanding this ruling requires appreciating the role of customary practice ('urf) and intention (niyyah) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband saying 'You are divorced like this' and pointing with fingers. The ruling is that divorce occurs, and the number is determined by the gesture.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith where the Prophet (s.a.w.) gestured with three fingers to indicate the length of a month (29 or 30 days). This establishes that finger gestures are recognized indicators of numbers in Shari'ah.
The hadith is used as evidence for the validity of gestures in determining the count.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's gesture with three fingers signifies the number three (or the number of days in a month, which is a number). By analogy, when a husband gestures with three fingers, it signifies three divorces.
4
Distinguish between diyanatan and qada'an
If the husband's intention differs from the apparent gesture (e.g., he gestures with three open fingers but intended a closed fist meaning one), the intention is accepted diyanatan (before Allah) but not qada'an (in court), because the court looks at apparent evidence.
The text gives an example: if he intends the closed fingers while using open fingers, diyanatan the number is one, but qada'an it is the apparent number (three).
5
Identify the default when no gesture
If the husband says 'You are divorced' without the word 'like this' (haka) and no gesture, only one divorce occurs because no number is specified.
The minimum number of divorces in one pronouncement is one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فى تشبيه الطلاق ووصفه ومن قال لامرأته انت طالق هكذا يشير بالابهام والسبابة والوسطى فهى ثلث لان الاشارة بالاصابع تفيد العلم بالعدد في مجرى العادة اذا اقترنت بالعدد المبهم قال عليه السلام الشهر هكذا وهكذا وهكذا. الحديث. وان انشار بواجدة فهى واحدة وان اشار بالثنتين فهى ثنتان لما قلنا والاشارة تقع بالمنشورة منها وقيل اذا اشار بظهورها فبالمضمومة منها واذا كان تقع الاشارة بالمنشورة منها فلونوى الاشارة بالمضمومتين يصدق ديانة لاقضاع وكذا اذا نوى الاشارة بالكف حتى يقع في الاولى ثنتان ديانة وفي الثانية واحدة لانه يحتمله لكنه خلاف الظاهر ولولم يقل هكذا يقع واحدة لانه لم تقترن
Chapter on simile of divorce and its description: And whoever says to his wife 'You are divorced like this,' pointing with thumb, index, and middle finger, then it is three, because pointing with fingers indicates knowledge of number in customary practice when accompanied by an ambiguous number. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' (i.e., three times). Hadith. And if he points with one, it is one; if he points with two, it is two, based on what we said. And pointing is done with the open (spread) fingers. And it is said: if he points with the back (of the hand), then with the closed (fingers). And if the pointing is with open fingers, if he intends the closed fingers, it is believed religiously (diyanatan) but not judicially (qada'an). Similarly, if he intends the palm, so that in the first case two fall diyanatan and in the second one, because it is possible, but it is against the apparent. And if he does not say 'like this', one divorce occurs because no (gesture) is associated.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling when a husband pronounces divorce using a gesture (tashbih) along with the words 'like this' (haka). The default ruling is based on the customary understanding of finger gestures. If he gestures with three fingers (thumb, index, middle), it counts as three divorces. This is supported by a hadith where the Prophet gestured with three fingers to indicate the number of days in a month. Similarly, pointing with one finger indicates one divorce, and with two fingers, two divorces. The gesture is understood as showing the number of divorces intended. If the husband gestures with a closed fist or back of hand, there are specific rulings: if he intends a particular number, that intention is accepted religiously (diyanatan, i.e., between him and Allah) but not in court (qada'an) because the apparent gesture differs. If he does not say 'like this' (haka), but simply gestures, the default is one divorce, because the gesture alone without the word 'haka' is ambiguous and the minimum is applied. The passage emphasizes that the gesture must be accompanied by the word 'haka' (like this) to count as a specific number; otherwise, it is treated as one divorce.

Key Terms
تشبیہ (tashbih): A simile or gesture used to indicate the number of divorces, such as saying 'like this' and pointing.
دیانتاً (diyanatan): Ruling according to what is between the person and Allah, based on his true intention, even if contrary to apparent evidence.
قضاءً (qada'an): Judicial ruling based on apparent evidence; court decides based on what is manifest, not hidden intentions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced like this' without any gesture, how many divorces occur?
A) None, because the statement is incomplete.
B) Three, because it is ambiguous.
C) One, because no gesture is associated.
D) The number depends on the husband's intention.

Key Takeaway
The apparent finger gesture, when accompanied by 'haka', determines the number of divorces; contrary intention is accepted diyanatan but not qada'an.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Divorce of a Slave Woman Freed Concurrently
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of divorce for a freed slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the timing of divorce when a slave woman is freed simultaneously, and apply the causal reasoning (talil) to determine the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Two divorces on a slave-girl are irrevocable even if she is freed simultaneously, because the divorce occurs in the state of slavery.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute where a master emancipates his slave-girl and simultaneously her husband divorces her twice. The key question: are the divorces revocable or irrevocable? We will explore the arguments of Imam Muhammad and the two shaykhs, focusing on the causal relationship between the pronouncement of divorce and its legal effect.

Guided Path
1
State the facts of the case
Identify the key elements: master says 'you are free', husband says 'you are divorced twice', at the same time. Determine the status of the woman (slave at the outset) and the legal question (are the divorces revocable or irrevocable?).
The case is distinct from one where talaq is suspended on the master's emancipation.
2
Identify the Hanafi positions
The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that the divorces are irrevocable (ba'in). Imam Muhammad holds they are revocable (raj'i). Understand the practical difference: revocable allows taking back during 'iddah; irrevocable does not.
Both positions are within the Hanafi school; the dispute is about the timing of the divorce relative to freedom.
3
Analyze Imam Muhammad's proof
Imam Muhammad argues that the emancipation part of the condition takes effect first, so the woman is free before the divorces apply. Thus the two divorces occur after freedom, making them revocable (since two divorces on a free woman are not irrevocable).
He relies on the sequence: the condition (emancipation) is partially fulfilled first.
4
Analyze the two shaykhs' proof
The shaykhs argue that talaq is suspended on the same event as 'itq; the event (the utterance of emancipation) occurs while she is still a slave, so the divorce also occurs in the state of slavery. Two divorces on a slave-girl cause irrevocable prohibition.
They emphasize the simultaneity of cause and effect: just as i'tiq is the cause of 'itq, tatliq is the cause of talaq.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its basis
The relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs. The reasoning is that causality makes the divorce simultaneous with the condition, not sequential. This is supported by the analogy of 'itq and talaq both being effects of their respective causes.
The text refutes Imam Muhammad's sequencing argument by asserting simultaneity.

امام محمد کی دلیل میں منتطلق بعد الحق غلط ہے۔ کیونکہ جس طرح اعتاق علت ہے عشق کی اسی طرح تطلیق علت ہے طلاق کی ۔ پس جب تطلیق عشق کے مقارن ہے تو طلاق بھی عشق کے مقارن ہوگی۔ لہذا وقوع طلاق اور وقوع حق دونوں ساتھ ساتھ ہیں اور وقوع عشق باندی ہونے کی حالت میں ہوا ہے۔ لہذا وقوع طلاق بھی باندی ہونے کی حالت میں ہوگا۔ اور باندی کیلئے دو طلاقیں حرمت غلیظہ ثابت کر دیتی ہیں۔ پس امام محمد کا اس دلیل کی روشنی میں طلاق رجعی کا قائل ہونا درست نہیں ہے۔ صاحب فتح القدیر نے امام محمد کی دلیل یہ ذکر کی ہے کہ مولیٰ کا قول انت حرة اور شوہر کا قول انت طالق ثنتین دونوں مجھی غد پر معلق ہیں۔ لہذا اعتاق اور تعلیق دونوں ایک زمانے میں پائے جائیں گے اور چونکہ انت حرة او جز ہے ( مختصر ) انت طالق مختین کے مقابلہ میں ۔ لہذا و جود میں او جز مقدم ہو گا یعنی انت حرة اورانت طالق منشین مؤخر ہوگا۔ تو گویا دو طلاقیں آزاد ہونے کے بعد واقع ہوئیں۔ اور آزاد ہونے کے بعد دو طلاق سے حرمت غلیظہ ثابت نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ اس وجہ سے امام محمد فر ماتے ہیں کہ اس صورت میں دو طلاق رجعی ہوں گی۔ شیخین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جس پر مولیٰ نے عشق کو معلق کیا ہے اس پر شوہر نے طلاق کو معلق کیا ہے اور محنق باندی ہونے کی حالت میں واقع ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا طلاق بھی باندی ہونے کی حالت میں واقع ہوگی۔ اور دو طلاقیں باندی پر حرمت غلیظہ ثابت کر دیتی ہیں۔ اس وجہ سے اس مسئلہ میں اس باندی کیلئے دو طلاقیں حرمت غلیظہ ثابت کر دیں گی۔ بر خلاف پہلے مسئلہ کے کیونکہ اس میں تطلیق کو اعتاق مولی پر معلق کیا گیا ہے تو گویا اعتاق شرط اور تعلیق جزاء ہے۔ اور جزاء شرط کے بعد واقع ہوتی ہے۔ لہذا یہاں طلاق عشق کے بعد واقع ہوگی۔ اور برخلاف عدت کے کیونکہ عدت میں احتیاط پر عمل کیا جاتا ہے۔ اور احتیاط تین حیض واجب کرنے میں ہے نہ کہ دو حیض ۔ اور حرمت غلیظہ میں بھی احتیاط کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ یہ عورت دو طلاقوں سے حرام بحرمت غلیظہ ہو جائے۔ اور امام محمد کی دلیل کا جواب دیتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ اگر عتق مقارن ہے اعتقاق کے کیونکہ اعتماق عنق کی علت ہے۔ تو طلاق مقارن ہے تطلیق کے۔ اسلئے کہ تعلیق طلاق کی علت ہے۔ لہذا اطلاق اور عتق دونوں میں اتصال ہوگا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
Imam Muhammad's proof in [the case of] 'mutlaq ba'd al-'itq' is incorrect. Because just as 'i'taq [emancipation] is the cause of 'itq [freedom], similarly tatliq [pronouncement] is the cause of talaq [divorce]. So when tatliq is simultaneous (muqarin) with 'itq, then talaq is also simultaneous with 'itq. Therefore, the occurrence of talaq and the occurrence of 'itq are together, and the occurrence of 'itq happened in the state of being a slave-girl. Therefore, the occurrence of talaq will also be in the state of being a slave-girl. And for a slave-girl, two divorces establish irrevocable prohibition (hurmat ghalizah). Thus, Imam Muhammad's view of revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) in light of this proof is not correct. The author of Fath al-Qadir mentioned Imam Muhammad's proof: the master's statement 'anti hurrah' and the husband's statement 'anti taliq thintain' are both suspended on the same condition. So emancipation and suspension occur at the same time, and since 'anti hurrah' is a part, it precedes 'anti taliq thintain'. So the two divorces occur after freedom. And after freedom, two divorces do not establish irrevocable prohibition. For this reason, Imam Muhammad says that in this case the two divorces will be revocable. The two shaykhs' proof: the husband suspended talaq on the same thing on which the master suspended 'itq, and its occurrence happens in the state of being a slave-girl. Thus, talaq also occurs in the state of being a slave-girl. And two divorces on a slave-girl establish irrevocable prohibition. Contrary to the first case because there tatliq was suspended on the emancipation of the master, so emancipation is the condition and suspension is the consequence. And the consequence occurs after the condition. Therefore, here talaq occurs after 'itq. And contrary to 'iddah because in 'iddah caution is observed, and caution requires three menstrual periods, not two. And also in irrevocable prohibition, caution requires that this woman becomes irrevocably prohibited by two divorces. And in answering Imam Muhammad's proof, [the shaykhs] said that if 'itq is simultaneous with i'tiq because i'tiq is the cause of 'itq, then talaq is simultaneous with tatliq because tatliq is the cause of talaq. So both 'itlaq and 'itq are connected. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the number and nature of divorces when a woman is a slave at the time of divorce but is freed simultaneously. The core issue: if a master says to his slave-girl 'you are free' and simultaneously the husband says 'you are divorced twice', are the divorces before or after her freedom? Imam Muhammad holds that the two divorces are revocable (raj'i) because they occur after freedom, while the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold they are irrevocable (ba'in) because the divorce is simultaneous with the emancipation, so she is still a slave at the moment of divorce. The text presents both arguments: Imam Muhammad's relies on the sequence, arguing that the emancipation part of the condition takes effect first, so she is free before the divorces apply. The shaykhs counter that the talaq is suspended on the same event as the 'itq, so they occur together, and the 'itq occurs in the state of slavery, hence talaq also occurs in slavery. The author then differentiates this case from two similar cases: (1) where talaq is suspended on the master's emancipation, then talaq occurs after freedom; (2) the 'iddah ruling, where caution demands three periods. The text concludes by refuting Imam Muhammad's proof, asserting that both 'itq and talaq are simultaneous because each is the cause of the other. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the shaykhain: two irreversible divorces.

Key Terms
تطلیق (tatliq): The act of pronouncing divorce; the verbal utterance of talaq.
حرمت غلیظہ (hurmat ghalizah): An irrevocable prohibition (severe unlawfulness), meaning the wife becomes completely forbidden to the husband without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the intra-Hanafi dispute about a slave woman who is freed and divorced simultaneously, what is the position of Imam Muhammad?
A) The two divorces are irrevocable (ba'in).
B) The two divorces are revocable (raj'i).
C) Only one divorce occurs.
D) The divorces are invalid.

Key Takeaway
When a slave woman is freed and divorced simultaneously, the two shaykhs' view prevails: the divorce occurs in the state of slavery, making two divorces irrevocably prohibiting.

Finger Gestures in Talaq: Determining the Number of Divorces
Explaining how finger gestures determine number of divorces in talaq with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of divorces when a husband gestures with fingers along with 'hakadha', and explain the distinction between qada' and diyana in such cases.

Core Idea
The number of divorces is determined by open fingers when 'hakadha' is spoken; intention for fewer is accepted only in conscience, not in court.

This passage from the sharh of Al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario in talaq: when a husband says 'anti taliq hakadha' (you are divorced like this) and gestures with his fingers. The number of divorces corresponds to the number of open fingers gestured. The text provides a proof from the Prophet's (PBUH) hadith about the month and discusses an intra-Hanafi difference regarding closed fingers. It also distinguishes between what is accepted in court (qada'an) and what is accepted in religious conscience (diyanatan) when the husband's intention contradicts the apparent gesture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband's statement 'anti taliq hakadha' accompanied by a finger gesture. The ruling is that the number of divorces equals the number of open fingers gestured.
This is the basic rule.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith where the Prophet (PBUH) said 'al-shahru hakadha wa hakadha wa hakadha' and gestured with his fingers to indicate the number of days in a month.
This shows that finger gestures customarily indicate numbers.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The Prophet used finger gestures to convey a number, so it is established that customarily finger gestures indicate numbers. Therefore, when attached to 'hakadha', the gesture specifies the number of divorces.
The hadith is of ahad transmission (zanni al-thubut) but its indication (dalala) is clear.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars hold that when pointing with the back of the fingers, closed fingers count. The author of Al-Hidayah prefers the view of open fingers. Identify the difference.
This affects counting when the husband points with the back of his hand.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the qada/diyana distinction
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the number is based on open fingers. If the husband intends a different number (e.g., two closed fingers), it is accepted in religious conscience (diyanatan) but not enforced in court (qada'an), because it contradicts the apparent gesture.
This distinction is crucial in fiqh.

Source Text

تشریح اگر کسی نے اپنی منکوحہ سے کہا انت طالق هكذا ـ اور انگوٹھے ، شہادت کی انگلی اور درمیان کی انگلی کیسا تھ اشارہ کرتا ہے تو اس صورت میں تین طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب اشارہ عدد مہم کے ساتھ متصل ہو تو عادتا انگلیوں سے اشارہ کر نا علم با اعدد کا فائدہ دیتا ہے۔ اور عدد جانے کیلئے انگلیوں سے اشارہ کافی ہے۔ اس کی تائید اس حدیث سے بھی ہوتی ہے کہ ایک مرتبہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے یہ بتلانے کیلئے کہ مہینہ ۲۹ کا بھی ہوتا ہے فرمایا الشهر هكذا وهكذا و هكذا ـ پہلی دوصورتوں میں دونوں ہاتھوں کی تمام انگلیوں سے اشارہ فرمایا اور تیسری مرتبہ میں ایک ہاتھ کے انگوٹھے کو بند کر لیا۔ اور اگر ھکذا کہہ کر ایک انگلی سے اشارہ کیا تو ایک واقع ہوگی اور اگر دو سے اشارہ کیا د واقع ہو جائیں گی ۔ سابقہ دلیل کی وجہ سے۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ طلاق کے عدد میں کھلی ہوئی انگلیوں کا اعتبار ہے۔ اور بعض حضرات کی رائے ہے کہ جب انگلیوں کی پشت سے اشارہ کیا تو عدد طلاق میں بند انگلیوں کا اعتبار ہوگا۔ فرماتے ہیں کہ جس صورت میں کھلی ہوئی تین انگلیوں سے اشارہ واقع ہوگا۔ اگر اس صورت میں دو بند انگلیوں سے اشارہ کی نیت کر لی یا ہتھیلی سے اشارہ کی نیت کر لی تو دیا تھا تصدیق کی جائے گی نہ کہ قضاء ۔ چنانچہ پہلی صورت میں دیا تھا دو واقع ہوں گی ۔ اور دوسری صورت میں ایک واقع ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ کلام بند انگلیوں سے اشارے کا۔ اور تھیلی سے اشارے کا احتمال رکھتا ہے لیکن خلاف ظاہر ہے اور یہ پہلے گذر چکا کہ اگر کلام خلاف ظاہر کا حتمال رکھتا ہو تو دیانتا معتبر ہو گا قضاء نہیں۔ اور اگر انت طالق کے بعد هكذا نہیں کہا اور تین انگلیوں سے اشارہ موجود ہے تو ایک ہی واقع ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ اشارہ عدد مہم کے ساتھ متصل نہیں ہے۔ پس صرف انت طالق کا اعتبار باقی رہ گیا اور اس سے ایک رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے۔ اسلئے ہم نے کہا کہ اس صورت میں ایک واقع ہوگی ۔
Translation: (This) chapter is about likening divorce and describing it. And a person who said to his wife 'you are divorced this many' and pointed with thumb, index finger, and middle finger, then these are three (divorces). Because pointing with fingers customarily gives knowledge of the number when the pointing is connected with the number. The Prophet (PBUH) said: The month is like this, and like this, and like this. The hadith. And if he pointed with one (finger), then one (divorce). And if he pointed with two (fingers), then two (divorces). Due to the proof we mentioned. And pointing occurs with open fingers. And it was said: when he pointed with the back of the fingers, then the pointing counts with closed fingers. And if he intended pointing with two closed (fingers), then it will be accepted in religious conscience, not in court. And similarly when he intended pointing with the palm. So in the first case, two (occur), and in the second case, one (occurs). Because the words can bear that meaning, but it is against the apparent. And if he did not say 'hakadha', then one occurs because the number is not connected with the ambiguous. So only his words 'anti taliq' remain in consideration. Commentary: If someone said to his wife 'anti taliq hakadha' and pointed with thumb, index, and middle finger, then in this case three divorces occur. The proof is that when pointing is connected with the ambiguous number, customarily pointing with fingers gives knowledge of the number. And pointing with fingers is sufficient for knowing the number. This is supported by the hadith that once the Prophet (PBUH) said 'the month is like this, and like this, and like this' to indicate that the month can be 29 days. In the first two instances he pointed with all fingers of both hands, and in the third he closed the thumb of one hand. And if he said 'hakadha' and pointed with one finger, then one occurs; if with two fingers, two occur. Due to the previous proof. The author of Hidayah says: In counting divorce, the consideration is of open fingers. And some scholars hold that when he pointed with the back of the fingers, then the count considers closed fingers. He says: In the case where pointing with three open fingers occurs, if he intends pointing with two closed fingers or pointing with the palm, then it will be accepted in religious conscience, not in court. Thus in the first case, two occur; in the second case, one occurs. Because the words bear the possibility of pointing with closed fingers or palm, but it is against the apparent. And it has passed before that if the words bear a possibility against the apparent, it will be accepted in religious conscience, not in court. And if he did not say 'hakadha' after 'anti taliq' and pointed with three fingers, then only one occurs. Because the pointing is not connected with the ambiguous number. So only the statement 'anti taliq' remains in consideration, from which one revocable divorce occurs. Therefore we said that in this case one occurs.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the sharh of Al-Hidayah. It explains the rule when a husband says to his wife 'anti taliq hakadha' (you are divorced like this) and gestures with his fingers. The number of divorces corresponds to the number of open fingers gestured, provided the gesture is connected to the word 'hakadha'. The proof is a hadith where the Prophet (PBUH) used finger gestures to indicate the number of days in a month. The text then distinguishes between open and closed fingers: the default is open fingers, but if the husband intends closed fingers or palm, that is accepted in religious conscience (diyanatan) but not enforced by a court (qada'an) because it contradicts the apparent gesture. If 'hakadha' is not said, then regardless of the gesture, only one revocable divorce occurs because the gesture is not explicitly connected to the number. The passage also notes a difference of opinion regarding whether closed fingers count when pointing with the back of the hand, but the relied-upon position is that open fingers are the basis.

Key Terms
ھكذا (hakadha): Word meaning 'like this', used to accompany a gesture indicating the number of divorces.
عدد مبہم (adad mubham): An ambiguous number that is clarified by the gesture.
دیانتا (diyanatan): In religious conscience, as opposed to court enforcement. If the husband's intention contradicts the apparent meaning, it is accepted only diyanatan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'anti taliq' and points with three open fingers without saying 'hakadha', how many divorces occur?
A) Three
B) One revocable
C) One irrevocable
D) None

Key Takeaway
The number of divorces is determined by open fingers only when 'hakadha' is said; without it, only one revocable divorce occurs, and the distinction between qada and diyana governs cases of hidden intention.

Divorce Described as 'Ba'in' or 'Al-Battah': Hanafi vs Shafi'i
States Hanafi ruling on using 'ba'in' or 'al-battah' adjectives in divorce and refutes Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce using 'ba'in'/'al-battah' after consummation, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument, and evaluate the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Describing divorce as 'ba'in' specifies an inherent meaning of irrevocability, not a contradiction.

When a husband says 'anti taliq ba'in' after consummation, does the divorce become irrevocable (ba'in) or revocable (raj'i)? The Hanafi school holds it is ba'in, while Imam al-Shafi'i argues it remains raj'i. This lesson unpacks the proof and counter-proof, showing how the Hanafi view relies on the inherent capacity of the word 'taliq' to carry irrevocability in some contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules when a husband says 'anti taliq ba'in' or 'anti taliq al-battah' after consummation.
The divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) even after consummation.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Name the opposing school (Imam al-Shafi'i) and state their ruling on the same scenario.
Shafi'i holds it is raj'i (revocable) after consummation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i argument: divorce after consummation is legislated to be revocable, so describing it as irrevocable is contrary to legislation and null, like saying 'anti taliq 'ala an la raj'ata li'.
The analogy is based on explicit negation of revocability.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the word 'taliq' itself can imply irrevocability in some contexts (before consummation, after iddah), so adding 'ba'in' merely specifies that meaning. The case of the condition phrase is different because it explicitly contradicts the legislation, whereas 'ba'in' does not.
The key distinction: adjective vs explicit condition.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence of the difference: under Hanafi law, the husband cannot revoke the divorce; under Shafi'i, he can.
This affects the husband's ability to take back his wife during iddah.

It (divorce) occurs as raj'i if after consummation, because divorce is legislated to be followed by revocability. So describing it with irrevocability is contrary to the legislation and thus null, as if he said: 'You are divorced with no revocability for me over you'. And for us, he has described it with something that its wording can bear. Do you not see that irrevocability before consummation and after the waiting period is attained by the word? So this description is to specify one of the two possibilities. And the case of revocability is not conceded. So one ba'in divorce occurs if he had no intention or intended two. But if he intended three, then three, as before. And if he meant by his saying 'You are divorced one' and by his saying 'ba'in' or 'al-battah' another, two ba'in divorces occur because this description is suitable for initiating the occurrence. Translation: And when divorce is described with an attribute of intensity and severity, it becomes ba'in, e.g. he said 'You are divorced ba'in' or 'al-battah'. Imam al-Shafi'i said: It is raj'i if after consummation, because divorce is legislated in a state where revocability follows it. So describing it with irrevocability is contrary to the legislation and thus null. Like when he said 'You are divorced on condition that I have no revocability over you'. Our proof: He described divorce with an attribute that its wording can bear. Do you not see that irrevocability before consummation and after the waiting period is attained by the word? So this description is to specify one of two possibilities. And the case of revocability is not conceded. So if he had no intention or intended two, one ba'in occurs. If he intended three, three occur, as per previous proof. And if he intended one by 'You are divorced' and another by 'ba'in' or 'al-battah', two ba'in occur because this description is suitable for initiating the occurrence. Explanation: If divorce is described with an attribute of intensity or severity, e.g. 'You are divorced ba'in' or 'al-battah', then the divorce is ba'in. Imam al-Shafi'i says it is raj'i if after consummation. His proof: Explicit divorce is legislated in a state where revocability follows, so making it irrevocable is contrary to legislation and void. Our proof: 'Taliq' is capable of irrevocability in some contexts. So the description specifies one of two possibilities. And the case of revocability (using the condition phrase) is not conceded; rather, if the husband has no intention or intends one or two, the statement yields one ba'in divorce. If he intends three, three occur. The proof has passed in the chapter of divorce. The author of Hidayah says: In 'You are divorced ba'in' or 'al-battah', if he intended one divorce by 'taliq' and another by 'ba'in' or 'al-battah', then two ba'in divorces occur. The proof is that 'ba'in' and 'al-battah' can initiate divorce. So just as if one says 'You are ba'in' with intention of divorce, divorce occurs, similarly here. And since the second divorce is ba'in, the first is also ba'in because two types cannot coexist in one woman.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding a husband who says to his wife 'anti taliq ba'in' (you are divorced irrevocably) or 'anti taliq al-battah' (you are divorced finally). The Hanafi position is that such a divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) even after consummation, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds it is raj'i (revocable). The commentary first presents the Shafi'i argument: divorce after consummation is legislated to be revocable, so describing it as irrevocable is contrary to the legislation and should be null, analogous to saying 'anti taliq 'ala an la raj'ata li 'alayki' (you are divorced on condition that I have no right to take you back). The Hanafi response is that the word 'taliq' itself carries the possibility of irrevocability in some contexts (before consummation or after the waiting period). Therefore, adding 'ba'in' or 'al-battah' merely specifies that the intended effect is immediate irrevocability, just as the word 'taliq' is capable of that meaning. The author then clarifies that if the husband had no specific intention or intended two divorces, one ba'in divorce occurs; if he intended three, three occur. Moreover, if the husband intends one divorce with 'anti taliq' and another separate divorce with 'ba'in' or 'al-battah', then two ba'in divorces occur because these words can independently initiate divorce. The reason both divorces are ba'in is that a woman cannot simultaneously be in a state of raj'i and ba'in with the same husband.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce that severs the marital tie immediately, after which the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage contract.
البتة (al-battah): An emphatic term meaning 'final, definitive', used to indicate an irrevocable divorce.
رجعی (raj'ī): Revocable divorce that allows the husband to take back his wife during her waiting period without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his wife after consummation 'anti taliq ba'in', what type of divorce occurs?
A) Raj'i (revocable) divorce
B) Ba'in (irrevocable) divorce
C) No divorce because the description is contradictory
D) A single ba'in divorce only if he intended it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, describing a divorce as 'ba'in' or 'al-battah' after consummation renders it irrevocable (ba'in) because the word 'taliq' itself carries the potential for irrevocability.

Talaq — Divorce

Intra-Hanafi Differences on Descriptive Phrases in Divorce: Ba'in or Raj'i?
Extends ba'in ruling to descriptive phrases; presents intra-Hanafi differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce phrased with adjectives like 'afhash al-talaq' and 'talaq al-bid'ah', and explain the intra-Hanafi differences regarding intention.

Core Idea
Descriptive adjectives in divorce can indicate irrevocability, but intention may matter for bid'ah and shaytan phrases.

This passage extends the previous ruling on 'ba'in' to other descriptive phrases such as 'most heinous divorce' and 'innovation divorce'. The general Hanafi position is that such phrases trigger an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce because they emphasize the effect of immediate separation. However, the passage then presents the views of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, who differ on whether intention is required or whether the description always implies irrevocability. Understanding this internal dispute deepens your grasp of how the Hanafi school balances literal meaning and intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the descriptive phrases
List the phrases covered: 'afhash al-talaq', 'akhabth al-talaq', 'aswa' al-talaq', 'talaq al-shaytan', 'talaq al-bid'ah'. Note that each describes the divorce in a way that emphasizes negativity.
These are additional to the phrase 'ba'in' itself.
2
State the general Hanafi position
According to the majority and the relied-upon view, uttering any of these phrases results in a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce, regardless of the husband's inner intention. The basis is the effect (athar) of the description: it points to immediate separation.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf narrates that for 'talaq al-bid'ah', the divorce is ba'in only if the husband intends it to be irrevocable. If he does not intend irrevocability, it remains raj'i. His reasoning: 'bid'ah' can refer to the timing (during menses) rather than the type of divorce.
This view requires examining the husband's intention.
4
Analyze Imam Muhammad's view
Imam Muhammad holds that for both 'talaq al-bid'ah' and 'talaq al-shaytan', the divorce is raj'i (revocable). His reasoning: the description might simply refer to the timing (during menses), so without certainty, irrevocability is not established due to doubt.
This is the most restrictive view regarding ba'in.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school (and the one reflected in Al-Hidayah) is that these phrases automatically trigger ba'in. The views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are noted as valid narrations but are not the operative fatwa.
The practical implication: a husband cannot later claim he intended raj'i to avoid ba'in.

للبدعہ میں کہ وہ بائن نہیں ہو گی مگر نیت کے ساتھ ۔ کیونکہ بدعت کبھی اس حیثیت سے ہوتی ہے کہ اس کو حالت حیض میں واقع کیا گیا ہے۔ لہذا نیت ضروری ہے۔ اور امام محمد سے مروی ہے کہ جب کہا انت طالق للبدعة باطلاق الشيطان تو رجعی ہوگی۔ کیونکہ کبھی یہ وصف متحقق ہوتا ہے حالت حیض میں طالق کے ساتھ لہذا شک کی وجہ سے بینونت ثابت نہیں ہوگی ۔ تشریح انت طالق انحش الطلاق چاروں احکام میں انت طالق بائن پر معطوف ہے۔ یعنی اگر کوئی نیت نہیں کی یادو کی نیت ہے ایک باسند واقع ہوگی ۔ اور گرتین کی نیت کی ہے۔ تو تین واقع ہوں گی۔ اور اگر انت طالق سے ایک کی نیت کی ہے اور افحش الطلاق سے دوسری ایک تو دود بائن واقع ہو جائیں گی ۔ یہی حکم ہے اخبث الطلاق اور اسوء الطلاق کا ۔ ان اوصاف کی وجہ سے طلاق بائن ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ طلاق اس وصف کے ساتھ متصف کی گئی ہے۔ اس کے اثر کے اعتبار سے اور وہ اثر فی الحال بینونت کا ہونا ہے۔ الہذا میدانت بائن کے مانند ہو گیا۔ اسی طرح اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے انت طالق طلاق الشيطان باطلاق البدعة کہا تو اس صورت میں بھی ایک طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ طلاق رجعی تو طلاق سنت ہے لہذا اطلاق بدعی اور طلاق شیطان ، طلاق بائن ہوگی۔ ہدایہ کی عبارت میں تھوڑا سا تساہل ہے وہ یہ کہ رجعی مطلقا سنی نہیں ہے بلکہ عام ہے اسلئے کہ اگر کسی نے حالت حیض میں لفظ صریحی سے طلاق دی تو وہ رجعی ہوگی۔ مگرسنی نہیں ہوگی۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ طلاق رجعی کیلئے سنی ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے امام ابو یوسف سے روایت ہے کہ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیگم سے کہا انت طالق
The ruling of divorcing by saying 'You are divorced with the most heinous divorce'. And similarly when he says 'You are divorced with the most heinous divorce' because he describes it with this description based on its effect, which is immediate irrevocability, so it becomes like his saying 'ba'in'. And similarly when he says 'the worst divorce' or 'the ugliest divorce' for the reason we mentioned. And similarly when he says 'the divorce of Satan' or 'the divorce of innovation' because the raj'i is the sunnah, so innovation and Satan's divorce are ba'in. And from Abu Yusuf regarding his saying 'You are divorced for innovation': it is not ba'in except with intention, because innovation may be from the perspective of occurrence during menses, so intention is necessary. And from Muhammad: when he says 'You are divorced for innovation' or 'Satan's divorce', it is raj'i because this description may be realized by divorce during menses, so irrevocability is not established due to doubt. Translation: And similarly (ba'in divorce occurs) when he says 'You are divorced with the most heinous divorce' because divorce is described with this description based on its effect, and that effect is immediate irrevocability, so it becomes like his saying 'ba'in'. And similarly when he says 'the worst divorce' or 'the ugliest divorce' for the reason we mentioned. And similarly when he says 'the divorce of Satan' or 'the divorce of innovation' because raj'i is sunnah, so innovation and Satan's divorce are ba'in. And from Abu Yusuf: in his saying 'You are divorced for innovation', it is not ba'in except with intention, because innovation may be from the perspective of occurrence during menses, so intention is necessary. And from Muhammad: when he says 'You are divorced for innovation' or 'Satan's divorce', it is raj'i because sometimes this description occurs with divorce during menses, so irrevocability is not established due to doubt. Explanation: 'You are divorced with the most heinous divorce' is attached to the rulings of 'You are divorced ba'in'. That is, if he had no intention or intended two, one ba'in occurs; if he intended three, three occur; and if he intended one with 'taliq' and another with 'afhash', two ba'in occur. The same ruling applies to 'akhabth' and 'aswaa'. The reason these descriptions make it ba'in is that divorce is described with these words based on their effect, which is immediate irrevocability. Thus, it is like 'ba'in'. Similarly, if someone says 'You are divorced with the divorce of Satan' or 'the divorce of innovation', then one ba'in divorce occurs. The proof is that raj'i divorce is the sunnah, so innovation and Satan's divorce are ba'in. There is a slight laxity in the text of Hidayah: raj'i is not necessarily sunni; it is general, because if someone divorces during menses with explicit words, it is raj'i but not sunni. So it is known that raj'i does not require being sunni. From Abu Yusuf: if someone says to his wife 'anti taliq'...
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling to other descriptive phrases. The author explains that if a husband says 'anti taliq afhash al-talaq' (you are divorced with the most heinous divorce), it is treated like saying 'ba'in', because the description points to the effect of immediate irrevocability. Similarly for 'akhabth al-talaq' (worst divorce) and 'aswa' al-talaq' (ugliest divorce). The same applies to 'talaq al-shaytan' (devil's divorce) and 'talaq al-bid'ah' (innovation divorce). The reasoning is that a raj'i divorce is the sunnah, so describing it as innovation or devil's work implies it is ba'in. However, there is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. According to a narration from Abu Yusuf, when a man says 'anti taliq lil-bid'ah' (you are divorced for innovation), the divorce is only ba'in if he intended it to be so, because innovation can also refer to the timing (during menses) without necessarily implying type. According to a narration from Imam Muhammad, such a divorce is raj'i because the description could simply refer to the timing (during menses), and since there is doubt, irrevocability is not established. The commentary then clarifies that these phrases follow the same four rulings as the previous case regarding intention (no intention → one ba'in, intention of three → three, etc.). It also notes a subtle point: the assertion that 'raj'i is sunnah' is not absolute, because a divorce during menses is raj'i but not sunnah; thus renewable divorce does not require being sunnah. The passage concludes with another transmission from Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
افحش الطلاق (afḥash al-ṭalāq): The most heinous type of divorce; a phrase used to emphasize the severity, which the Hanafis interpret as indicating irrevocability.
طلاق البدعة (ṭalāq al-bidʿah): Innovation divorce; a divorce that is contrary to the sunnah in its timing or manner, such as divorcing during menses.
طلاق الشيطان (ṭalāq al-shayṭān): Satanic divorce; a term used to describe a divorce that is unlawful or causes harm, similar in effect to bid'ah divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad (as narrated in this passage), what is the ruling if a husband says 'anti taliq lil-bid'ah'?
A) It is ba'in regardless of intention
B) It is raj'i because the description could refer to timing rather than type
C) It is ba'in only if the husband intends three divorces
D) No divorce occurs because the phrase is unclear

Key Takeaway
Descriptive phrases in divorce, such as 'afhash al-talaq' and 'talaq al-bid'ah', generally result in an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce, although Abu Yusuf and Muhammad have nuanced views on the role of intention for bid'ah and shaytan phrases.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Ruling on Saying 'Anta Taliq Afhash al-Talaq' and Similar Phrases
States ruling on bain divorce via extreme descriptions and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that describing divorce by its effect (bain) produces a bain divorce, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi positions on 'talaq al-bid'a'.

Core Idea
Describing divorce by its effect (bain) makes it bain — no intention needed.

In this passage, the Hidayah commentator explains that certain extreme descriptions of divorce, such as 'afhash al-talaq' (the most obscene divorce), automatically produce a bain (irrevocable) divorce because they describe the divorce by its immediate effect of permanent separation. This is similar to explicitly saying 'bain'. The passage then presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding phrases like 'talaq al-bid'a' (divorce of innovation) and 'talaq al-shaytan', where the majority holds they are bain, but Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad have differing views. Understanding this ruling helps you apply the key principle that describing an act by its legal effect can determine the outcome without requiring explicit intention for that effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what divorce utterance is being analyzed and what the immediate ruling is. The passage deals with saying 'anta taliq afhash al-talaq' and similar phrases, and the ruling is that one bain divorce occurs.
Note that no intention for the number of divorces is needed; it is one bain by default.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is qiyas (analogy) to explicitly saying 'bain'. The commentator states that describing divorce by its effect (immediate irrevocability) makes it like saying 'bain', so the same ruling applies.
The key phrase is 'fasaara kaqawlihi bain' (it becomes like his saying bain).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage records disagreement among the Hanafi imams regarding 'talaq al-bid'a' and 'talaq al-shaytan'. The majority (Abu Hanifa) says it is bain; Abu Yusuf says it requires intention for bain; Muhammad says it is raj'i.
Understand the reasoning: Abu Yusuf considers that 'bid'a' could refer to timing (during menses), so intention is needed. Muhammad argues that doubt prevents establishing bain.
4
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The commentary upholds the majority view (bain) as the relied-upon position for fatwa. This is because the matn's explicit ruling and the principle that description by effect suffices without intention.
The tarjih authority is the Hidayah and its commentary, which follow the majority.

Divorces will be bain (irrevocable). The evidence is that the word 'bain' and the word 'al-batta' can initiate divorce. Thus if someone said to his wife 'anti bain' with intention of divorce, divorce occurs. Similarly here, taking a second divorce as bain is correct. And since the second divorce is bain, the first is also bain, because a woman cannot have both raj'i and bain divorce simultaneously. Ruling on divorcing by saying 'anta taliq afhash al-talaq'. Likewise if he says 'anta taliq afhash al-talaq' because it is described with this description due to its effect, which is immediate irrevocability, so it becomes like his saying 'bain'. Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the Hidayah commentary, explaining that phrases like 'afhash al-talaq', 'akhbath al-talaq', etc., imply an immediate bain (irrevocable) divorce because they describe the divorce by its effect—permanent separation. The author analogizes these to the word 'bain' itself. The passage then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning 'talaq al-bid'a' and 'talaq al-shaytan'. The majority view (based on the matn) is that these are bain, as raj'i is sunna and opposite of bid'a. However, Abu Yusuf holds that 'li'l-bid'a' requires explicit intention for bain, because bid'a can refer to timing (during menses). Imam Muhammad holds it is raj'i because the description could refer to the timing, and bain is not established by doubt. The commentary further explains the application: if one says 'anta taliq afhash al-talaq' without intention, it is one bain; if he intends two, it is two bain; if he intends three, three. The same applies to similar phrases. The teacher should note that the Hanafi school relies on the principle that describing divorce by its effect (bain) makes it irrevocable, and that the ruling for 'afhash' and similar is one bain divorce unless intention for more is proven.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): An irrevocable divorce that terminates the marriage immediately, without right of return except through a new marriage.
رجعی (raj'ī): A revocable divorce where the husband may return to his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.
افحش الطلاق (afḥash al-ṭalāq): Literally 'the most obscene of divorces', a phrase used to emphasize the severity, resulting in bain divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) No divorce occurs because it is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Phrases describing divorce by its effect (bain) result in a bain divorce without need for intention, though ikhtilaf exists for 'talaq al-bid'a' and 'talaq al-shaytan'.

Divorce by Comparison to a Mountain: Bain or Raj'i?
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that tashbih (comparison) in divorce implies increase in description (bain) unless the comparison is purely quantitative, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counterargument.

Core Idea
Comparing divorce to a mountain makes it bain because the increase must be in description, not number.

This lesson examines a disputed divorce phrase from the Hanafi school: when a husband says to his wife 'anti taliq ka'l-jabal' (you are divorced like a mountain). The majority of the school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) rules that this results in one bain (irrevocable) divorce, while Abu Yusuf holds it is a single raj'i (revocable) divorce. The key lies in understanding what the comparison adds: the mountain's oneness or its permanence?

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and the dispute
The husband says 'anti taliq ka'l-jabal' (you are divorced like a mountain). The majority says bain; Abu Yusuf says raj'i. Note the two opposing positions.
2
Analyze the majority's qiyas
The majority likens this phrase to saying 'anti taliq bain' (explicit bain) via qiyas. The illa (effective cause) is that tashbih necessarily implies an increase (ziyada). Since the mountain is one entity, the increase cannot be in number, so it must be in description (strength/permanence), making it bain.
Qiyas jaliyy: the asl is explicit bain; the far' is the mountain comparison; the illa is the increase in description.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that the comparison is only in oneness (the mountain is one), so it merely affirms a single divorce without any additional description. Thus it should be raj'i.
This is a disagreement over the nature of tashbih: quantitative vs qualitative.
4
Follow the majority's response
The majority counters that since a mountain is a single entity, an increase in number is impossible; therefore the tashbih must carry a qualitative increase—description—which results in bain.
The majority's position is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa).
5
Apply the principle to similar phrases
The same ruling applies to 'mithl al-jabal' (like the mountain). The principle extends to any tashbih that cannot be merely quantitative, e.g., 'like a rock' would similarly be bain.
This tests understanding of the illa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ پہاڑشی ، واحد ہے۔ لہذا پہاڑ کے ساتھ تشبیہ اس کے ایک ہونے میں ہوگی۔ یعنی جس طرح پہاڑ ایک ہے اسی طرح تجھ کو طلاق ایک ہے لہذا اس تشبیہ سے طلاق بائن کا ہونا لازم نہیں آتا۔
Likewise if he says 'like a mountain' because likening to it necessarily causes increase, and that is by affirming increase of description. Likewise if he says 'like the mountain' for what we said. Abu Yusuf said it is raj'i, because a mountain is one thing, so comparing her to it is in its oneness. Translation: And similarly (will be bain) when he says (anti taliq) ka'l-jabal. The evidence of the majority is that likening to a mountain causes increase, and increase can be in number or description. A mountain does not admit increase in number, so it must be in description, and description increase is bain, so it is bain. Abu Yusuf's evidence: the mountain is one, so the comparison is in its oneness, meaning just as the mountain is one, your divorce is one, so it does not necessitate bain.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling_with_proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'anti taliq ka'l-jabal' (you are divorced like a mountain). The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad according to the matn) holds that this results in one bain divorce. Their reasoning: comparing the divorce to a mountain necessarily implies an 'increase' (ziyada). Since a mountain is a single entity, the increase cannot be in number, so it must be in description—the description of the divorce as being strong and permanent, i.e., bain. Therefore, it is a bain divorce. Abu Yusuf argues that the comparison is simply in oneness: just as the mountain is one, so is the divorce one. Thus it is raj'i (one revocable divorce). The teacher should highlight the underlying principle: when a comparison (tashbih) is made, it imports an additional meaning. If the object of comparison is singular, the addition is taken as qualitative (strength/permanence) unless proven otherwise. Abu Yusuf's view is based on literal oneness. The mufta bihi is the majority position (bain), as it aligns with the general rule that tashbih increases description.

Key Terms
تشبیہ (tashbīh): A simile or comparison that imparts an additional meaning to the divorce, such as strength or permanence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the majority's ruling that 'anti taliq ka'l-jabal' is bain?
A) The mountain is huge, so it implies many divorces.
B) The tashbih requires an increase, and since the mountain is one, the increase must be in description (irrevocability).
C) Abu Yusuf's opinion is ignored.
D) The word 'jabal' is similar to 'jafal' (palm tree) which is a custom.

Key Takeaway
When a divorce phrase uses tashbih to a singular entity, the default Hanafi ruling is that it results in a bain divorce due to qualitative increase.

Ruling on 'Ashadd al-Talaq', 'Ka'l-Alf', and 'Mil' al-Bayt'
Ruling on ambiguous divorce phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for 'ashadd al-talaq', 'ka'l-alf', and 'mil' al-bayt', explain the effect of intention, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Ambiguous divorce phrases default to one bain divorce; intention for three is valid but must be proven.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses three specific phrases a husband might use to divorce his wife: 'ashadd al-talaq' (the most severe divorce), 'ka'l-alf' (like a thousand), and 'mil' al-bayt' (filling the house). Each phrase can carry multiple meanings, and the Hanafi school provides clear rules to determine how many divorces occur. The commentary also presents intra-Hanafi differences between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and establishes the default ruling when no intention is proven.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three phrases and their ambiguity
Recognize that each of the three phrases ('ashadd al-talaq', 'ka'l-alf', 'mil' al-bayt') can have multiple interpretations: severity vs. irrevocability, strength vs. number, greatness vs. quantity. This ambiguity triggers the need for default rules.
The court must determine the husband's intention if possible; otherwise, the default applies.
2
Apply the default rule for ambiguous phrases
In the absence of proven intention, the lesser meaning is established. For each phrase, this means one bain divorce occurs. This is the mufta bihi position.
The default of one bain is based on the principle of taking the smaller number (al-akhadh bi al-aqall) when a phrase can mean either one or multiple.
3
Understand the role of intention
If the husband intended a specific number (e.g., three), his intention is valid because the word 'talaq' (masdar) was explicitly mentioned. The phrase is sarih (explicit), allowing the divorce to be counted as intended.
Intention for three must be proven; it is not assumed.
4
Learn the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'ka'l-alf'
Imam Muhammad held that 'ka'l-alf' automatically results in three divorces because 'alf' (thousand) is a number and the apparent meaning is numerical. Abu Yusuf held that if greatness is not mentioned, the divorce is raj'i. Abu Hanifa maintained the default of one bain.
The commentary follows Abu Hanifa's principle as the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).
5
Grasp the general principle on tashbih
Abu Hanifa stated that any comparison (tashbih) in divorce results in bain because it implies an increase of description, making the divorce permanent. Abu Yusuf restricted bain to cases where greatness is specifically mentioned.
The difference stems from whether the attribute of 'severity' is inherent in all comparisons or only explicit statements.

And if he said to her 'anta taliq ashadd al-talaq' or 'ka'l-alf' or 'mil' al-bayt', then it is one bain unless he intends three. As for the first: because he described it with severity, and that is the bain because it does not admit rescission and revocation, while raj'i does. And the intention of three is valid because he mentioned the masdar (the word talaq). As for the second: because sometimes this tashbih is intended for strength and sometimes for number. It is said 'he is like a thousand men' meaning strength. So intention of either is valid, and in absence of intention, the lesser is established. And from Muhammad that three occurs in absence of intention because it is number, so the tashbih is apparently in number, so it becomes like saying 'anta taliq ka'adad alf'. As for the third: because something can fill the house due to its own greatness or due to its quantity. So whichever he intends, his intention is valid; in absence of intention, the lesser is established. Then the principle with Abu Hanifa: whenever he compares divorce to something, it falls bain regardless of what the thing compared is, whether he mentioned greatness or not, because tashbih necessitates increase of description. And with Abu Yusuf: if he mentions greatness it becomes bain, otherwise not, whatever the thing compared is, because...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the Hidayah commentary, addressing three specific phrases and the general principle. First, 'ashadd al-talaq' (the most severe divorce) is like 'bain' because severity implies irrevocability. Intention for three is valid because 'talaq' is mentioned (as a generic noun). Second, 'ka'l-alf' (like a thousand) can mean strength or number; without intention, the lesser (one bain) is established. Imam Muhammad holds it is three by default because the apparent meaning is number. Third, 'mil' al-bayt' (filling the house) can mean greatness or quantity; without intention, the lesser (one bain) is established. The passage concludes with the general principle: according to Abu Hanifa, any tashbih (comparison) in divorce results in bain because tashbih implies increase of description. According to Abu Yusuf, only if greatness is mentioned does it become bain; otherwise it is raj'i. The teacher should emphasize that the mufta bihi is the default of one bain divorce for these ambiguous phrases, and that intention for more is valid but must be proven.

Key Terms
اشد الطلاق (ashadd al-ṭalāq): The most severe divorce, meaning bain (irrevocable) as opposed to raj'i.
مصدر (maṣdar): Here, the verbal noun 'talaq' itself; mentioning the masdar allows intention for multiple divorces.

Talaq with Simile to a Mountain – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on talaq with simile to a mountain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on talaq pronounced with a simile to a mountain (ka al-jabal) and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding whether it is bain or raj'i.

Core Idea
When talaq is compared to a singular object, the Hanafi school holds it implies an increase in quality (baynunah), making it bain—not raj'i.

In Hanafi fiqh, the wording of divorce can affect its legal consequence. A husband might say, 'You are divorced like the mountain.' Does this make the divorce irrevocable (bain) or revocable (raj'i)? The school is divided: the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rule bain, while Abu Yusuf rules raj'i. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, saying 'anti taliq ka al-jabal' or 'mithl al-jabal' results in one bain divorce.
Bain divorce ends the marriage immediately and requires a new marriage contract for reconciliation.
2
Identify the opposing position
Abu Yusuf holds that the same wording results in one raj'i divorce, which allows reconciliation during the iddah without a new contract.
Raj'i divorce leaves the door open for return during the waiting period.
3
Follow the reasoning of the majority
The simile to a mountain necessitates an increase (ziyadah). Since the mountain is singular, the increase cannot be in number, so it must be in attribute. The attribute intended is baynunah (irrevocability).
The increase in attribute is the key: the mountain's greatness is transferred to the quality of the divorce.
4
Follow the reasoning of Abu Yusuf
The simile is in the mountain's oneness (wahdah). Since both the divorce and the mountain are single, the comparison indicates a single divorce without implying an increased attribute. Hence the divorce remains raj'i.
Abu Yusuf sees the simile as purely numerical: one thing compared to one thing.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the husband says 'anti taliq ka al-jabal' without any intention, the ruling differs: according to the majority, it is bain; according to Abu Yusuf, it is raj'i. This affects the possibility of halal reconciliation.
In fatwa practice, the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is generally relied upon.

And likewise, when he said 'like a mountain', because the comparison to it necessitates an increase without doubt, and that is by establishing an increase in the attribute. And likewise when he said 'like a mountain' due to what we said. And Abu Yusuf said it would be raj'i, because the mountain is one thing, so the comparison of her to it is in its oneness. Translation: And similarly (it becomes bain) when he said 'anti taliq ka al-jabal'. Because the comparison to the mountain certainly necessitates an increase, and this increase occurs by establishing the attribute. And similarly when he said 'mithl al-jabal' due to the proof we mentioned. And Abu Yusuf said that it would be raj'i, because the mountain is a single thing. So the comparison to it is in its oneness. Explanation of the issue: A man said to his wife 'anti taliq ka al-jabal' or 'mithl al-jabal', then according to the two (i.e., Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) one bain divorce occurs, and according to Abu Yusuf one raj'i divorce occurs. The proof of the two is that the comparison to the mountain necessitates an increase, and increase is of two types: in number and in attribute. The mountain does not admit increase in number because it is singular in itself, so the increase in attribute is intended, and the increase in attribute is baynunah, so bain divorce occurs. The proof of Abu Yusuf is that the mountain is a single thing, so the comparison to it is in its oneness, i.e., just as the mountain is one, similarly your divorce is one, so bain divorce is not necessitated.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage begins with a broader principle: Talaq al-bid'ah is sometimes due to baynunah (making it irrevocable) and sometimes due to pronouncing it during menses. For baynunah to be established, explicit intent is needed. Imam Muhammad narrates that if one says 'anti taliq li al-bid'ah' or 'anti taliq talaq al-shaytan', raj'i divorce occurs because the description could also be true due to giving divorce during menses (without baynunah), so doubt prevents baynunah. Then the text focuses on the simile of a mountain. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that saying 'anti taliq ka al-jabal' or 'mithl al-jabal' results in one bain divorce. Their reasoning: comparison (tashbih) to a mountain necessitates an increase, and since the mountain is singular in number, the increase must be in attribute—namely baynunah, which makes the divorce irrevocable. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the comparison is in oneness (the mountain is one), so it indicates a single raj'i divorce, not bain. The commentary explains both positions.

Key Terms
تشبیہ (tashbih): Simile or comparison; here it is used in the context of pronouncing divorce by comparing it to something else (like a mountain).
بینونت (baynunah): Irrevocability in divorce; a bain divorce ends the marriage immediately without the possibility of taking back except through a new marriage.
رجعی (raj'i): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back the wife during the iddah without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One raj'i divorce occurs.
C) Three divorces occur.
D) No divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
When a simile to a singular object is used in talaq, the Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rules it as one bain divorce because the increase must be in attribute.

Talaq with Extreme Expressions and Similes
Rulings on three talaq expressions and intra-Hanafi dispute on similes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for each of the three expressions (ashadd al-talaq, ka al-f, mil' al-bayt) and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding similes in talaq.

Core Idea
In Hanafi talaq, similes cause one bain divorce unless the husband intends three; imams differ on the role of the word 'greatness'.

When a husband uses extreme descriptions or similes in pronouncing divorce, the Hanafi school has detailed rules. This lesson examines three specific expressions—'ashadd al-talaq', 'ka al-f', and 'mil' al-bayt'—and the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Zufar, and Muhammad regarding whether the divorce is bain or raj'i. Understanding these nuances helps you apply the correct ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three expressions
Recognize the phrases ashadd al-talaq, ka al-f, mil' al-bayt and their default rulings (one bain divorce).
The passage distinguishes each based on the nature of the description.
2
Determine the nature of the simile
For each expression, understand whether the simile is about strength, number, greatness, or abundance. This affects the default ruling and the acceptance of intention.
Ka al-f can mean strength or number; mil' al-bayt can be greatness or abundance.
3
Apply the principle of intention
If the husband intends a specific number (especially three), his intention is valid and the divorce occurs accordingly. If no intention, the lesser (one bain) is established except in Muhammad's view for ka al-f.
Intention of three is accepted because the verbal noun (masdar) is mentioned.
4
Compare the imams' general principles
Abu Hanifa: any simile leads to bain. Abu Yusuf: only if 'greatness' is mentioned. Zufar: if the object is commonly considered great. Muhammad: two narrations (with Abu Hanifa or Abu Yusuf).
The disagreement centers on whether the word ʿiẓam is necessary for baynunah.
5
Compute the practical difference
For a given simile without 'greatness', determine the ruling according to each imam (bain vs raj'i). This clarifies the thamarah al-khilaf.
Example: 'anti taliq ka al-jabal' without ʿiẓam.

بہر حال اول سو اسلئے کہ طلاق کو متصف کیا ہے شدت کے ساتھ اور وہ (شدید ) بائن ہے۔ کیونکہ بائن ٹوٹنے اور چھوٹنے کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی ہے۔ بہر حال رجعی سو وہ اس کا احتمال رکھتی ہے۔ اور تین کی نیت صحیح ہوگی اس کے مصدر کو ذکر کرنے کی وجہ سے۔ اور بہر حال ثانی تو اسلئے کہ کبھی اس سے وقت میں تشبیہ مراد ہوتی ہے۔ اور کبھی عدد میں ۔ کہا جاتا ہے ھو الف رجل اور مراد اس سے قوت ہوتی ہے۔ پس دونوں امروں کی نیت کرنا صحیح ہے۔ اور نیت کے مفقود ہونے کے وقت ... ان دونوں میں کا اول ثابت ہوگا۔ اور امام محمد سے مروی ہے کہ عدم نیت کے وقت تین واقع ہوں گی ۔ کیونکہ الف عدد ہے۔ پس اس سے ظاہراً تشبیہ فی العددمراد ہو گی ۔ پس ہو گیا جیسا کہ جب کہا انت طالق کعد دالف اور بہر حال تیسرا تو اسلئے کہ شی، کبھی گھر کو بھر دیتی ہے اپنے بڑا ہونے کی وجہ سے اور کبھی بھر دیتی ہے اپنی کثرت کی وجہ سے پس جو بھی نیت کرے گا اس کی نیت صحیح ہوگی اور نیت کے معدوم ہونے کے وقت اقل ثابت ہوگا۔ پھر ضابطہ ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ ہے کہ جب طلاق کو کسی چیز کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی گئی تو طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی ۔ مشبہ بہ کچھ بھی ہو ( لفظ ) عظم مذکور ہو ی مذکور نہ ہو۔ کیونکہ گذر چکا کہ تشبیہ زیادتی وصف کا تقاضا کرتی ہے۔ اور ابو یوسف کے نزدیک اگر ( لفظ ) عظم مذکور ہے تو بائن ہوگی ورنہ نہیں مشبہ یہ کچھ بھی ہو۔ کیونکہ تشبیہ بھی ایک ہونے میں ہوتی ہے عظمت کے معنی سے خالی کر کے۔ بہر حال عظم کا ذکر تو یقیناً زیادت کیلئے ہے۔ اور امام زفر کے نزدیک اگر مشبہ بہ ان چیزوں میں ہے جس کو بڑا ہونے کے ساتھ موصوف کیا جا سکتا ہے لوگوں کے نزدیک تو بائن واقع ہوگی۔ ورنہ تو وہ رجعی ہے۔ اور کہا گیا کہ امام محمد ابو حنیفہ کیساتھ ہیں اور کہا گیا کہ ابو یوسف کے ساتھ ہیں۔ اور اس اختلاف کا ظہور اس کے قول مثل رأس الابره الخ میں ہے۔ یعنی سوئی کے ناکے کے مثل سوئی کے ناکے کی بڑائی کے مثل اور پہاڑ کے مثل ، پہاڑ کی بڑائی کے مثل ۔ . تشریح اس عبارت میں تشبیہ کی تین مثالیں ذکر کی گئی ہیں۔ ان تنیوں کا حکم یہ ہے کہ طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی۔ اور اگر تین کی نیت کی تو تین واقع ہو جائیں گی صاحب ہدایہ تینوں کو مدیل فرمارہے ہیں۔ چنانچہ اول کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ انت طالق اشد الطلاق میں طلاق کو وصف شدت کے ساتھ متصف کیا ہے۔ اور دیہ شدت بینونت کے ذرریعہ حاصل ہوگی کیونکہ بیونت کے بعد طلاق ٹوٹنے پھوٹنے کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی ہے۔ اس وجہ سے ہم نے کہا کہ اس لفظ سے طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی البتہ اگر نیت ثلاث پائی گئی تو تین واقع ہو جائیں گی کیونکہ اس شخص نے مصدر طلاق کا ذکر کیا ہے تقدیری عبارت ہے انت طالق طلاقا ھو اشد الطلاق ۔ اور مصدر اسم جنس ہونے کی وجہ سے بغیر وصف شدید کے تین کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ پس وصف شدت کے ساتھ تو بدرجہ اولیٰ تین کا احتمال رکھے گا۔ اور دوسری مثال یعنی انت طالق كالف ۔ سو اس سے طلاق بائن اسلئے واقع ہوگی کہ کبھی کالف سے قوت میں تشبیہ دینا مراد ہوتا ہے اور بھی عدد میں جیسے لوگ کہتے ہیں ہوالف رجل وہ ایک ہزار آدمیوں کے برابر ہے۔ یہاں قوت میں تشبیہ دینا مراد ہے۔ بہر حال اس کلام
And if he said to her: 'You are divorced with the most severe divorce', or 'like a thousand', or 'filling the house', then it is one bain divorce unless he intends three. As for the first, because he described it with severity, and severity is bain because it does not admit being broken or annulled, whereas raj'i admits that. And the intention of three is valid because he mentioned the verbal noun. As for the second, because the comparison can sometimes be in strength and sometimes in number. It is said 'he is like a thousand men' meaning strength. So intention of either is valid, and when intention is absent, the lesser is established. From Muhammad it is narrated that three occur when there is no intention, because 'alif' is a number, so the comparison is apparently in number, so it becomes like when he said 'like the number of a thousand'. As for the third, because something can fill the house due to its own greatness or due to its abundance. So whatever he intends is valid, and when intention is absent, the lesser is established. Then the principle according to Abu Hanifa is that whenever divorce is compared to something, one bain divorce occurs, no matter what the thing is, whether 'greatness' is mentioned or not, because comparison necessitates increase in attribute. According to Abu Yusuf, if 'greatness' is mentioned, it is bain; otherwise not, no matter what the thing is, because comparison can be in oneness and abstraction; but mentioning 'greatness' is certainly for increase. According to Zufar, if the thing compared is such that it is described as great among people, then bain; otherwise raj'i. It is said that Muhammad is with Abu Hanifa, and it is said he is with Abu Yusuf. And the clarification of this difference is in his statement: like the head of a needle, like the greatness of the head of a needle, and like a mountain, like the greatness of a mountain. Translation: And if a woman is told 'anti taliq ashadd al-talaq' or 'ka al-f' or 'mil' al-bayt', then one bain divorce occurs unless he intends three. As for the first, because he described the divorce with severity, and severity is bain because it does not admit being broken, whereas raj'i admits that. And intention of three is valid because he mentioned the verbal noun. As for the second, because sometimes the simile is in strength and sometimes in number. It is said 'huwa alfu rajul' meaning strength. So intention of both is valid, and when intention is absent, the lesser is established. And from Muhammad it is narrated that three occur when there is no intention, because 'alif' is a number, so the comparison is apparently in number, so it becomes like when he said 'like the number of a thousand'. As for the third, because something may fill the house due to its own greatness or due to its abundance. So whatever he intends, his intention is valid, and when intention is absent, the lesser is established. Then the principle of Abu Hanifa is that when divorce is compared to something, one bain divorce occurs, no matter what the thing is, whether the word 'greatness' is mentioned or not, because the comparison necessitates increase in attribute. According to Abu Yusuf, if 'greatness' is mentioned, it is bain; otherwise not, no matter what the thing is, because comparison can be in oneness without abstraction; but mentioning 'greatness' is certainly for increase. According to Zufar, if the thing compared is among things that can be described as great in people's view, then bain; otherwise raj'i. And it is said that Muhammad is with Abu Hanifa, and it is said he is with Abu Yusuf. And the clarification of this difference is in his statement: 'like the head of a needle, like the greatness of the head of a needle, and like a mountain, like the greatness of a mountain.' Explanation: In this expression, three examples of comparison are mentioned. The ruling for these three is that one bain divorce occurs. And if he intended three, then three occur. The author of al-Hidayah supports all three. Thus the proof for the first is that in 'anti taliq ashadd al-talaq', divorce is described with severity, and severity is attained through baynunah because after baynunah the divorce does not admit being broken. Therefore we said that from this word one bain divorce occurs. However, if intention of three is found, then three occur because the person mentioned the verbal noun (masdar) of talaq; the implied phrase is 'anti taliq talaqan huwa ashadd al-talaq'. And since the verbal noun is a noun of genus, it admits the possibility of three even without the description of severity; with the description of severity, it a fortiori admits three. For the second example 'anti taliq ka al-f' – one bain divorce occurs because sometimes the simile is in strength and sometimes in number, as people say 'huwa alfu rajul' meaning he is like a thousand men in strength. Here the simile is in strength. Anyway, this speech...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage deals with three specific expressions: 'ashadd al-talaq' (most severe divorce), 'ka al-f' (like a thousand), and 'mil' al-bayt' (filling the house). The default ruling is that each of these causes one bain divorce, unless the husband intends three divorces, in which case three occur. The text then explains the reasoning for each: For 'ashadd al-talaq', the word 'ashadd' (most severe) describes the divorce itself; severity in talaq is baynunah (irrevocability) because a bain divorce cannot be revoked, while a raj'i divorce can be. If he intends three, it is valid because he implied a verbal noun (talaqan), which can indicate the number. For 'ka al-f', it can mean strength (like a thousand men) or literal number; without intention, the lesser (one) is established, but Imam Muhammad says it is three because 'alif' is a number, so the simile is in number by default. For 'mil' al-bayt', the filling could be due to greatness (e.g., a big object) or abundance (e.g., many small objects); without intention, the lesser (one) is established. Then the text outlines the general principles of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar regarding any simile in talaq. Abu Hanifa: any simile results in bain because it implies an increase in attribute. Abu Yusuf: only if the word 'greatness' (عظم) is mentioned; otherwise raj'i. Zufar: if the object compared is commonly described as great among people, then bain; otherwise raj'i. The text notes that there are two narrations about Imam Muhammad's position (with Abu Hanifa or with Abu Yusuf). The examples given (head of a needle, mountain, with/without 'greatness') illustrate the differences.

Key Terms
اشد الطلاق (ashadd al-talaq): The most severe divorce; by default it means one bain divorce because severity is associated with irrevocability.
کالف (ka al-f): Like a thousand (literally 'like the thousand'). Used in simile; can indicate strength or number depending on context.
ملء البیت (mil' al-bayt): Filling the house; a simile indicating abundance or greatness of the filler.
عظم (ʿizam / ʿazm): Greatness/bigness; when mentioned in the simile, it explicitly indicates an increase in attribute, leading to bain divorce according to Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'anti taliq ka al-f' without any intention, what is the ruling according to Imam Muhammad?
A) One bain divorce occurs.
B) One raj'i divorce occurs.
C) Three divorces occur.
D) No divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
Three expressions all yield one bain divorce by default in Hanafi, but the imams differ on the condition for baynunah in general similes.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
Rulings for Exaggerated Divorce Expressions (Ashadd, Ka-Alif, Mal' al-Bayt)
Explains rulings and proofs for three exaggerated divorce phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the three exaggerated divorce phrases and apply the principle of aqall (minimum) when intention is absent.

Core Idea
Exaggerated divorce phrases yield one ba'in divorce unless three are intended; ambiguity defaults to the lesser.

This lesson examines three specific divorce expressions that imply intensity or completeness: 'ashadd al-talaq', 'ka-alif', and 'mal' al-bayt'. The Hanafi school treats each as producing a single ba'in divorce if no intention of three is present, based on linguistic analysis and the principle of taking the minimum in case of doubt. The lesson also covers an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding 'ka-alif' and the general rule of tashbih according to the leading Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Phrases
List the three expressions: 'anti taliq ashadd al-talaq', 'anti taliq ka-alif', and 'anti taliq mal' al-bayt'. Recognise that each contains a form of tashbih (comparison) or exaggeration.
These are not ordinary talaq; they add qualitative intensity.
2
Analyse the Linguistic Feature
For each phrase, determine the specific tashbih: 'ashadd' is a superlative, 'ka-alif' compares to 'alif' (meaning either strength or number 1000), and 'mal' al-bayt' describes filling the house (greatness or multiplicity).
The tashbih adds a quality that makes the divorce ba'in.
3
Determine the Default Ruling
According to the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and one narration from Muhammad), when intention is absent, each phrase results in one ba'in divorce. The key reasoning is that ba'in is appropriate for severity, and when meanings are ambiguous, the minimum (aqall) is taken.
Three divorces only occur if the husband explicitly intended three.
4
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ka-Alif
Imam Muhammad holds (in a different narration) that 'ka-alif' without intention yields three divorces because 'alif' is a number (1000), making the tashbih numerical. The majority rebuts that 'alif' also means strength, so ambiguity is resolved by the lesser.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: one vs three divorces.
5
Apply the General Rule of Tashbih
The text provides a rule: according to Abu Hanifa, any tashbih makes the divorce ba'in; according to Abu Yusuf, only if the word 'azm (greatness) is mentioned; according to Zufar, only if the object compared is inherently great in people's view. Apply this to the examples.
The majority opinion (Abu Hanifa and one view of Muhammad) is relied upon.
