Definition and Ruling of Kinayah (Implied Divorce)
Defines kinayah and states its ruling requiring intention or indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of kinayah in talaq and explain the condition of intention or contextual indication for its validity.

Core Idea
Kinayah words require intention or context to effect talaq; without them, divorce does not occur.

Having covered explicit (sarih) divorce, the author now turns to kinayah—words that are not specifically coined for divorce but can imply it. This lesson unpacks the ruling that such ambiguous utterances require either the husband's specific intention (niyyah) or a clear contextual indication (dalalah hal) to be legally effective. Understanding this principle is essential for distinguishing between valid and invalid implied divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term: kinayah
Recognize that kinayah words are those not explicitly placed for divorce, e.g., 'you are free' or 'go away.'
2
Understand the requirement for validity
Note that such words do not cause divorce by themselves; they need niyyah (intention) or dalalah hal (contextual indication).
3
Grasp the rationale
The ambiguity of these words (they can mean divorce or something else) necessitates specification to remove uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم ثانی ... کنایات کا بیان الفاظ کنائی میں نیت یا دلالت حال سے طلاق واقع ہوتی ہے اما الضرب الثاني وهو الكنايات لايقع بها الطلاق الابالنية او بدلا لة الحال لانها غير موضوعة للطلاق بل تحتمله و غیره فلابد من التعيين او دلالته ترجمہ اور بہر حال قسم ثانی وہ کنایات ہیں ان کے ذریعہ طلاق واقع نہیں ہو گی ۔ مگر نیت کے ساتھ یا دلالت حال کے ساتھ ۔ کیونکہ کنابات طلاق کیلئے موضوع نہیں ہیں۔ بلکہ طلاق اور غیر طلاق دونوں کا احتمال رکھتے ہیں۔ پس تعیین یا دلالت تعیین ضروری ہے۔ تشریح مصنف جب قسم اول یعنی صریح الطلاق کے بیان سے فارغ ہو گئے تو قسم ثانی یعنی کنایات کو بیان فرمارہے ہیں ۔ کنایہ وہ ہے جس کی مراد منتر اور مخفی ہو۔ اور کنایہ کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس پر عمل کرنا واجب نہیں ہو گا ۔ مگر یہ کہ نیت پائی جائے یا نیت کے قائم مقام دلالت حال وغیرہ پائی جائے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ الفاظ کنایہ صرف طلاق کیلئے وضع نہیں کیئے گئے ہیں بلکہ طلاق اور غیر طلاق دونوں کا احتمال رکھتے ہیں ۔ لہذا نیت کے ساتھ ایک معنی کو متعین کرنا ضروری ہے یا کم از کم تعیین معنی کیلئے دلالت پائی جائے۔
Second section ... statement of kinayah. By implied words, divorce occurs only with intention or contextual indication (dalalah hal). As for the second type, which is kinayah, divorce does not occur by them except with intention or with contextual indication, because they are not placed for divorce but can imply divorce or other, so specification or indication is necessary. Translation: And in any case, the second type is kinayah; by them divorce does not occur except with intention or with contextual indication, because kinayah words are not coined for divorce, rather they can imply divorce or other, so specification or indication for specification is necessary. Explanation: When the author finished explaining the first type, which is explicit divorce (sarih al-talaq), he now explains the second type, i.e., kinayah. Kinayah is that whose meaning is hidden and concealed. The ruling of kinayah is that acting upon it is not obligatory unless there is intention or what stands in place of intention, like contextual indication. The proof is that kinayah words are not placed only for divorce, but they can imply divorce or other, so it is necessary to specify one meaning with intention, or at least a contextual indication for specification of meaning.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement about kinayah (implied words) in divorce. The author transitions from sarih (explicit) talaq to kinayah. Kinayah refers to words that are not originally coined for divorce but can be interpreted as divorce depending on context (e.g., 'you are freed', 'go away'). The key ruling is that such words do not effect divorce by themselves; they require either the husband's specific intention (niyyah) to divorce, or a contextual indication (dalalah hal) that clearly shows he intended divorce. The reason is that these words are ambiguous—they can mean divorce or other things like freeing a slave or telling someone to leave. Therefore, specification (ta'yin) is necessary to remove uncertainty. This principle is foundational in Hanafi law for distinguishing between explicit and implied expressions in legal acts.

Key Terms
کنایات (kinayat): Plural of kinayah. Words that are not explicitly coined for divorce but can imply it, such as 'you are free', 'you are on your own', etc.
دلالت حال (dalalah hal): Contextual indication or circumstantial evidence that clarifies the speaker's intent, such as the state of anger or the presence of a dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for talaq to occur using kinayah (implied) words?
A) Only the husband's utterance
B) The husband's specific intention or a contextual indication
C) The presence of witnesses
D) The wife's acceptance

Key Takeaway
Kinayah words do not automatically cause divorce; they require niyyah or dalalah hal.

Effect of Waw and Fa' in Conditional Divorce: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Imam Abu Hanifa's proof and dispute with Sahibayn on conjunction in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Abu Hanifa holds that only one divorce occurs when using 'waw' in a conditional divorce, and evaluate the Sahibayn's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Doubt in the meaning of 'waw' prevents additional divorces in conditional talaq.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute over the number of divorces when a husband pronounces 'You are divorced one and one' under a condition. The disagreement hinges on the linguistic ambiguity of the conjunction 'waw' (و) and the application of the principle that doubt (shakk) prevents the occurrence of divorce. By examining Imam Abu Hanifa's reasoning and the Sahibayn's analogy, you will understand how Hanafi usul is applied to resolve such delicate issues.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Imam Abu Hanifa holds regarding the number of divorces when 'waw' is used in a conditional divorce.
Imam Abu Hanifa: only one divorce occurs.
2
Identify the opposing Sahibayn position
State the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn) on the same issue.
Sahibayn: two divorces occur sequentially.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
Understand the analogy (qiyas) they make to the case where the condition is delayed (takhir al-shart).
Analogy: in delayed condition, both divorces fall after condition; here condition precedes, so they occur sequentially.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine Imam Abu Hanifa's refutation: distinguish between precedence and delay, and the principle of doubt.
In precedence, first statement is not suspended; wife is non-consummated so first makes her bain, preventing second.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical difference in the number of divorces and the resulting marital status.
Abu Hanifa: one revocable divorce. Sahibayn: two divorces (but second ineffective).

امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مطلقاً جمع اتصال اور ترتیب دونوں کا احتمال رکھتی ہے۔ پس اگر اتصال کے معنی کی رعایت کی جائے تو دو واقع ہوں گی۔ اور اگر ترتیب کے معنی کا لحاظ کیا جائے تو ایک واقع ہو گی۔ جیسا کہ انت طالق واحدة وواحدة طلاق منجز میں صرف ایک واقع ہوگی ۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ ایک طلاق سے زائد میں شک ہے اور شک کی وجہ سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوتی ۔ اسلئے ایک سے زیادہ واقع نہیں ہوگی۔
صاحبین نے تقدم شرط کی صورت کو تاخیر شرط کی صورت پر قیاس کیا ہے ہم کہتے ہیں کہ یہی قیاس صحیح نہیں۔ کیونکہ تاخیر کی صورت میں شرط اول کلام کو بدل دینے والی ہے۔ لہذا اول کلام شرط پر موقوف رہے گا۔ پس وجود شروط کے بعد دونوں طلاقیں دفعہ واقع ہو جائیں گی۔ اور تقدیم کی صورت میں اول کلام کو بدل دینے والی کوئی چیز موجود نہیں۔ پس اول کلام، آخر کلام پر موقوف بھی نہیں ہوگا۔ اس وجہ سے دونوں طلاقیں علی الترتیب واقع ہوں گی۔ اور عورت چونکہ غیر مدخول بہا ہے اسلئے وہ واحدہ اولیٰ سے بائنہ ہو جائے گی اور ثانیہ واقع نہیں ہو گی۔ اسی طرح انت طالق مختین پر قیاس کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ مشین کی تصریح کے وقت ترتیب کا احتمال ہی نہیں رہا۔ اور حروف واؤ کے ساتھ ترتیب کا احتمال ہے۔ پس اس فرق کے ہوتے ہوئے قیاس کرنا ، قیاس مع الفارق ہوگا۔ اور اگر حرف فاء کے ساتھ عطف کیا۔ مثلاً کہا انت طالق واحدة فواحدة پس امام کرخی کے بیان کے مطابق یہ بھی مختلف فیہ ہے۔ یعنی امام صاحب کے نزدیک ایک اور صاحبین کے نزدیک دو واقع ہوں گی۔ گویا امام کرتی نے عطف بالواو اور عطف بالفاء کو برابر کر دیا۔ حالانکہ یہ میچ نہیں ۔
اور فقیہ ابواللیث فرماتے ہیں کہ عطف بالفاء کی صورت میں بالاتفاق ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی اور یہی اصح ہے کیونکہ فاء تعقیب یعنی ترتیب حکم پر دلالت کرنے کیلئے آتا ہے۔
Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the word 'waw' absolutely carries the probability of both simultaneity (connection) and sequence. So if the meaning of simultaneity is observed, then two divorces occur. And if the meaning of sequence is considered, then one divorce occurs. Similarly, in the case of 'You are divorced one and one' in immediate divorce, only one divorce occurs. So it is known that there is doubt regarding more than one divorce, and due to doubt, divorce does not occur. Therefore, more than one will not occur. The Sahibayn analogized the case where the condition precedes (i.e., the condition is mentioned first) to the case where the condition is delayed (i.e., mentioned later). We say that this analogy is not correct. Because in the delayed case, the condition changes the first statement. Hence, the first statement will remain suspended upon the condition. So after the existence of the condition, both divorces occur simultaneously. And in the case of precedence, there is nothing present to change the first statement. So the first statement will not be suspended upon the last statement. Therefore, both divorces will occur sequentially. And since the woman is non-consummated, she becomes irrevocably divorced by the first 'one' and the second does not occur. Similarly, analogizing it to 'You are divorced if you enter the house' is not correct. Because when the condition is specified, there remains no probability of sequence. And with the letter waw, there is a probability of sequence. So, with this difference, making an analogy is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). And if the conjunction is with the letter fa', for example, he said 'You are divorced one, then one', then according to the statement of Imam al-Karkhi, this too is disputed. That is, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, one, and according to the Sahibayn, two will occur. As if Imam al-Karkhi equated conjunction with waw and conjunction with fa'. Although this is not matching. And Faqih Abu al-Layth says that in the case of conjunction with fa', by consensus one divorce occurs, and this is more correct because fa' indicates immediate sequence (ta'aqub), i.e., sequence of ruling.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the conjunction waw (و) when used in conditional divorce pronouncements. The issue is whether the waw implies simultaneity (jam') or sequence (tartib), and how many divorces occur when a man says, for example, 'You are divorced one, and one' under a condition. The author opens by stating Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: the waw linguistically is ambiguous, bearing both meanings. If understood as simultaneity, two divorces fall; if sequence, only one. Since there is doubt, the ruling favors fewer divorces (one). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) analogize to a case where the condition is stated after the divorce (takhir al-shart), but the author points out a distinction: when the condition is delayed, it suspends the entire first statement, so both divorces occur simultaneously after the condition. When the condition precedes (taqdim al-shart), the first statement is not suspended, so divorces occur sequentially. However, since the wife is non-consummated, the first makes her irrevocably divorced, and the second does not take effect. The author refutes the Sahibayn's analogy by noting that in waw there is probability of sequence, unlike in a clear conditional statement (e.g., 'int taliq in dakhalti'). Then the discussion turns to conjunction with fa', where it is said there is disagreement among early scholars, but Faqih Abu al-Layth holds that by consensus only one divorce occurs because fa' clearly indicates sequence.

Key Terms
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; the dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
عطف بالواو (ataf bil-waw): Conjunction using the letter waw (and); its impact on the number of divorces is disputed due to ambiguity.
شک (shakk): Doubt; in Hanafi fiqh, shakk prevents the occurrence of divorce (principle: al-shakk la yuzilu al-yaqin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, how many divorces occur when a non-consummated wife is told 'anti taliq wahidah wa wahidah' under a condition?
A) Two divorces occur
B) One divorce occurs
C) Three divorces occur
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce with the conjunction 'waw', the Hanafi school follows Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that only one divorce occurs due to the principle that doubt prevents additional divorces.

Kinayah Divorce: Definition and Three Words of Revocable Divorce
Definition of kinayah divorce and explanation of three kinayah words that effect one revocable divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kinayah in Hanafi divorce law and identify three kinayah words that effect one revocable divorce, explaining the requirement of intention or context.

Core Idea
Kinayah words require intention or context to effect divorce; three specific words always yield one revocable divorce when intended.

Divorce in the Hanafi school is not limited to explicit words like 'Anti taliq.' Certain allusive expressions (kinayah) can also bring about divorce, but only if the husband's intention or the surrounding context makes the meaning clear. This lesson will define kinayah and then examine three key words that, when used with the proper intent, result in one revocable divorce. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying Hanafi divorce law.

Guided Path
1
Define kinayah and contrast with sarih
Understand that kinayah words are not coined exclusively for divorce; they carry multiple possible meanings, unlike explicit words. This distinction underlies the requirement for intention or context.
Sarih words like 'Anti taliq' are unambiguous; kinayah words require specification.
2
Identify the legal requirement for kinayah divorce
Recognize that kinayah words effect divorce only with intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalah al-haal). Without either, no divorce occurs.
The text states: 'لايقع بها الطلاق الابالنية او بدلا لة الحال'.
3
List the three specific kinayah words
Memorize the three words: 'اعتدی' (I'ddi), 'استبرئی رحمک' (Istabri'i rahimak), and 'انت واحدة' (Anti wahidah). These are the only kinayah words that, when intended, yield one revocable divorce.
The passage says 'ثلثة الفاظ يقع بها طلاق رجعى'.
4
Analyze the reasoning for each word
For each word, trace the double meaning and why intending the divorce‑related meaning implies a prior divorce and thus revocability. For example, 'I'ddi' can mean counting the 'iddah (implying divorce) or counting blessings.
The text provides the rationale for each word, linking it to the concept of 'iddah and return (raj'ah).
5
Explain why only one divorce occurs
Understand that the implicit phrase 'Anti taliq' is either implied or embedded, and even if explicit would yield only one divorce. The word 'wahidah' explicitly specifies a single divorce, contradicting any intention of three.
The text notes that specifying 'one' (واحدة) negates an intention of three.

قال وهي على ضربين منها ثلثة الفاظ يقع بها طلاق رجعى ولاتقع بها الاواحدة وهي قوله اعتدى و استبرئ رحمك و انت واحدة اما الاولى فلانها تحتمل الاعتداد عن النكاح و تحتمل اعتداد نعم الله تعالى فان نوى الأول تعين بنية فيقتضى طلاقا سابقا والطلاق يعقب الرجعة واما الثانية فلانها تستعمل بمعنى الاعتداد لانه تصريح بما هو المقصود منه فكا منزلته وتحتمل الاستبراء ليطلقها واما الثالته فلانها تحتمل ان تكون نعتالمصدر محذوف معناه تطليق احدة فاذانواه جعل كانه قاله والطلاق يعقب الرجعة وتحتمل غيره وهوان تكون واحدة عنده او عند قومه ولما احتملت هذه الالفاظ الطلاق وغيره يحتاج فيه الى النية ولا يقع الاواحدة لان قوله انت طالق فيها مقتضى او مضمر ولو كان مظهر الاتقع بها الا واحدة فاذا كان مضمرا اولى وفي قوله واحدة ان صار المصدر مذكور الكن التنصيص على الواحدة ينافي نية الثلث ولا معتبر با عراب الواحدة عند عامة المشائخ وهو الصحيح لان العوام لايميزون بين وجوه الاعراب
Second section: Statement of Kinayah. Divorce occurs by kinayah words only with intention or contextual indication, because they are not coined for divorce but bear the probability of divorce and other meanings. Therefore specification (ta'yin) or its indication is necessary. ... He said: And they are of two types; from among them, three words by which revocable divorce occurs, and only one occurs, and they are his saying: 'I'ddi (count the 'iddah)', 'Istabri'i rahimak (seek purity of your womb)', and 'Anti wahidah (you are one).' As for the first, because it bears the meaning of counting the 'iddah from marriage and the meaning of counting the blessings of Allah, so if he intends the first, it becomes specified by intention, thus it requires a prior divorce, and divorce is followed by return. As for the second, because it is used in the meaning of passing the 'iddah because it expresses what is intended from it, so it is like its status, and it bears the meaning of seeking purity to divorce her. As for the third, because it bears the meaning of being an attribute of a deleted infinitive, its meaning being 'one divorce,' so when he intends that, it becomes as if he said it, and divorce is followed by return; and it also bears other meanings, such as she is unique in his eyes or among his people. Since these words bear divorce and other meanings, intention is needed, and only one occurs because his saying 'Anti taliq' is either implied or implicit. If it were explicit, only one would occur; when it is implicit, it is more so. And in his saying 'wahidah,' even though the infinitive is mentioned, specifying one contradicts the intention of three. And the declension of 'wahidah' is not considered according to the general mashayikh, and that is correct because common people do not distinguish between types of declension.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage introduces the second category of divorce: kinayah (allusive/implicit) words. The author first defines kinayah: they are words not originally coined for divorce but can be used to imply it, requiring either explicit intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalah al-haal) to effect divorce. The reason is that they carry multiple meanings, so specification is necessary. Then the passage details three specific kinayah words that, when used with intention, effect one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). These are: (1) I'ddi (count the 'iddah): it can mean counting the 'iddah after marriage (implying divorce) or counting Allah's blessings (not divorce). If the husband intends the former, it becomes a revocable divorce. (2) Istabri'i rahimak (seek purity of your womb): it is used as a substitute for 'iddah and implies divorce; it can also mean seeking purity to divorce later. (3) Anti wahidah (you are one): it is an adjective for a deleted infinitive, so it means 'one divorce.' It can also mean she is unique. In all these, the word 'anti taliq' is implicit, and only one divorce occurs because specifying 'one' contradicts an intention of three. The declension of 'wahidah' is irrelevant as common people don't distinguish.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kinayah): An allusive expression that can mean divorce or other things; requires intention or context to effect divorce.
اعتدی (i'taddi): The command 'count the 'iddah'; one of the three kinayah words that effect revocable divorce.
استبرئی رحمک (istabri'i rahimak): The command 'seek purity of your womb'; a kinayah word that can mean observing 'iddah or seeking purity for divorce.
انت واحدة (anti wahidah): The phrase 'you are one'; a kinayah meaning 'you are divorced one time' if intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Kinayah divorce requires intention or context; three specific words ('I'ddi, Istabri'i rahimak, Anti wahidah) effect one revocable divorce when intended.

Three Kinayah Words Causing One Revocable Divorce
Explaining the three specific kinayah words and their ruling with proof and dispute resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three kinayah words that cause a revocable divorce, explain why only one divorce occurs, and apply the Hanafi ruling on the irrelevance of i'rab in 'Anti wahidah'.

Core Idea
Three kinayah words yield only one revocable divorce because the implicit divorce phrase cannot produce more, and i'rab of 'wahidah' is irrelevant.

This passage from Al-Hidayah explains a key distinction in Hanafi divorce law: among kinayah (allusive) expressions, three specific words produce exactly one revocable divorce when divorce is intended. These words—I'taddi, Istabri'i rahimaki, and Anti wahidah—each have multiple possible meanings, so intention is required. The passage also resolves a dispute about the case ending of 'wahidah', asserting the majority view that i'rab does not affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
List the three words and their dual meanings
Identify each word (I'taddi, Istabri'i rahimaki, Anti wahidah) and the two possible interpretations: one related to divorce and one unrelated.
This shows why intention is needed.
2
Apply the rule of intention
Recognize that kinayah words require niyyah to effect divorce. If the husband intends divorce, one revocable divorce occurs.
Without intention, no divorce.
3
Trace the proof: implicit phrase limits number
Understand the dalil: these words imply 'Anti taliq'. If explicit, only one divorce occurs; so when implied, it is even more restricted to one.
This is a logical proof (dalil aqli).
4
Resolve the objection about 'Anti wahidah'
Even though the infinitive 'talaq' is implied, the specification of 'one' prevents intending three. Hence only one divorce.
The word 'wahidah' itself blocks the intention of three.
5
Apply the i'rab rule
The case ending (nasb, raf', sukun) of 'wahidah' does not affect the ruling. This is the correct view because common people cannot distinguish i'rab.
This resolves the intra-Hanafi dispute.

رحمک اور انت واحدۃ ہے۔ بہر حال (کلمہ ) اولی تو اسلئے کہ وہ احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ نکاح سے شمار کرنے کا اور اللہ کی نعمتوں کو شمار کرنے کا۔ پس اگر اول کی نیت کی تو اس کی نیت سے ( یہ معنی متعین ہو گئے ۔ پس تقاضا کرے گا طلاق سابق کا۔ اور طلاق کے بعد رجعت ہوتی ہے۔ اور بہر حال دوسرا ( کلمہ ) اس لئے کہ وہ مستعمل ہے عدت گذرنے کے معنی میں ۔ کیونکہ یہ (کلمہ ثانیہ ) اس چیز کی صراحت ہے۔ جو عدت سے مقصود ہے۔ پس ہو گا بمنزلہ اعتداد کے۔ اور احتمال رکھتا ہے استبراء کا تاکہ اس کو طلاق دے سکے۔ اور بہر حال (کلمہ ) ثالثہ تو وہ احتمال رکھتا ہے یہ کہ مصدر محذوف کی صفت ہو۔ اس کے معنیٰ ہوں تطلیقۃ واحدۃ ۔ پس جب اس کی نیت کی تو بنا دیا گیا گویا کہ کہاوہ اور طلاق کے بعد رجعت ہوتی ہے۔ اور اس کے غیر کا بھی احتمال رکھتی ہے۔ وہ یہ کہ عورت یکتا ہو اس کے نزدیک یا اس کی قوم کے نزدیک اور جب یہ الفاظ طلاق اور غیر طلاق کا احتمال رکھتے ہیں تو ( طلاق کے معنی مراد لینے میں محتاج نیست ہوگا۔ اور صرف ایک واقع ہوگی۔ اسلئے کہ اس کا قول انت طالق ان الفاظ میں مقتضی ہے یا مضمر ہے۔ اور اگر (اس کا قول انت طالق ) ظاہر ہوتا تو اس سے صرف ایک واقع ہوتی۔ پس جب مقدر ہے تو بدرجہ اولی ( ایک واقع ہوگی اور اس کے قول واحدة میں اگر چہ رچه مصدر مذکور ہے لیکن واحدۃ کی تصریح تین کی نیت کے منافی ہے۔ اور عام مشائخ کے نزدیک واحدہ کے اعراب کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ اور یہی صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ عوام وجود اعراب کے درمیان امتیاز نہیں کر سکتے ۔

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد چہارم
شمار کر۔ پس اگر شوہر نے معنی اول کی نیت کی تو اس کی نیت سے معنی اول متعین ہو جائیں گے۔ اور یہ معنی طلاق کے متقدم ہونے کا تقاضا کرے گا۔ کیونکہ امر بالاعتداد بغیر طلاق کے بیچ نہیں ہے۔ لہذا اطلاق کو سابقا مقدم ما نا ضروری ہے۔
اور دوسراکلمہ (استبرائی رحمک) سو اس میں بھی دو کا احتمال ہے ایک یہ کہ عدت گزار تو کیونکہ عدت گذرنے سے مقصود ہی رحم کو پاک کرنا ہے تو گویا مقصود عدت کو صراحتاذ کر کر دیا گیا ہے۔ پس اسقبر کی رحمک ، اعتدی کے مرتبہ میں ہوگا۔ اور دوسرے معنی یہ ہیں کہ حیض سے رحم کو پاک کرنا کہ مسنون طریقہ پر طلاق دی جا سکے۔ اس صورت میں بھی اگر معنی کیا نیت کی تو اس کی نیت سے معنی اول متعین ہو جائیں گے ۔ اور یہی معنی طلاق سابق کا مقتضی ہوگا۔ اور رہا تیسراکلمہ یعنی انت واحدة وں میں بھی دو معنی کا احتمال ہے۔ اول یہ کہ واحدة کو مصدر محذوف کی صفت قرار دیا جائے۔ یعنی تطلیقہ واحدۃ ۔ اور دوسرے معنی یہ کہ شوہر اس کی تعریف کرتا ہوا کہتا ہے کہ تو میرے نزدیک زمانہ میں یکتا ہے۔ یا تو میری قوم میں یکتا ہے۔ یعنی تجھ جیسی کوئی دوسری عورت نہیں ہے۔ پس اس صورت میں بھی اگر معنی اول کی نیت کی
تو گویا اس نے انت تطلیقۃ واحدۃ کہا اور اس کلام سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔ لہذا انت واحدۃ سے بھی ایک ربعی واقع ہوگی ۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ جب یہ الفاظ ثلاثہ طلاق اور غیر طلاق دونوں کا احتمال رکھتے ہیں تو معنی طلاق مراد لینے کیلئے نیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے۔ اور ان الفاظ سے ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہو گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ اول کے دو لفظوں یعنی اعتدی اور استمبر کی رحمک میں انت طالق اقتضاء ثابت ہے۔ اور تیسرے کلمہ یعنی انت واحدۃ میں طلاق مقدر ہے۔ اوداس کا قول انت طالق یا تطلیقہ اگر ظاہر ہوتا تو صرف ایک طلاق واقع ہوتی۔ پس جب لفظ طلاق مقدر ہے تو بدرجہ اولی ایک واقع ہوگی۔
و في قوله واحدة سے ایک اشکال کا جواب ہے۔ اشکال یہ ہے کہ انت واحدۃ میں مصدر طلاق مذکور ہے ۔ پس تین طلاقوں کی نیت کرنا صحیح ہونا چا. ہونا چاہئے ۔ جواب واحدۃ کی تصریح تین کی نیت کے منافی ہے۔ اس وجہ سے واحدۃ کے ساتھ تین کی نیت کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ انت واحدۃ میں واحدۃ کے اعراب کا اعتبار نہیں کیا گیا ہے واحدۃ منصوب پڑھا جائے یا مرفوع یا سکون کے ساتھ تینوں صورتوں میں طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ عام مشائخ کی یہی رائے ہے اور یہی صحیح ہے۔ اور بعض مشائخ کی رائے یہ ہے کہ اگر واحدۃ نصب کے ساتھ ہے تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اگر چہ طلاق کی نیت نہ کی ہو۔ اور اگر رفع کے ساتھ ہے تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی اگر چہ طلاق کی نیت کی ہو۔ اور اگر سکون کے ساتھ پڑھا ہے تو محتاج نیت ہوگا۔ صحیح اول ہے کیونکہ عوام اعراب کی صورتوں میں امتیاز کرنے پر قادر نہیں ہیں ۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
He said: And they are of two types. Three of them are words by which a revocable divorce occurs, and only one occurs. They are his words: 'I'taddi', 'Istabri'i rahimaki', and 'Anti wahidah'. As for the first, because it can mean counting the 'iddah from marriage or counting the blessings of Allah. If he intends the former, it is determined by intention, and it necessitates a prior divorce, and divorce is followed by return (raj'a). As for the second, because it is used in the sense of completing the 'iddah, as it is explicit in what is intended from it, so it is like 'I'taddi', and it can also mean istibra' to divorce her. As for the third, because it can be an adjective of an omitted infinitive, meaning 'one divorce', so if he intends that, he becomes as if he said it, and divorce is followed by return, and it can also mean other things: that she is unique in his view or in his people's view. Since these words can imply divorce or other things, intention is needed, and only one divorce occurs because his words 'Anti taliq' are inherent or implied in them. If it were explicit, only one would occur, so if implied, it is more so. And in his word 'wahidah', even though the infinitive is mentioned, specifying 'one' negates the intention of three, and the i'rab of 'wahidah' is not considered according to the general mashayikh, and this is correct because common people cannot distinguish between forms of i'rab. Translation: And kinayah are of two types. Three of them are words from which a revocable divorce occurs, and only one occurs. They are his words: I'taddi, Istabri'i rahimaki, and Anti wahidah. Explanation: The first (word) is because it can mean counting the days of 'iddah or counting Allah's blessings. If he intends the first, the first meaning is determined by his intention, and it necessitates a prior divorce because the command to observe 'iddah is meaningless without divorce, so it is necessary to assume divorce occurred before. The second word (Istabri'i rahimaki) also has two possibilities: one is to spend the 'iddah, because the purpose of 'iddah is to purify the womb, so it is like stating the purpose explicitly, thus it is on the level of 'I'taddi'; the other meaning is to purify from menses in order to give divorce in the prescribed manner. In this case, if he intends the first meaning, it is determined and necessitates a prior divorce. The third word (Anti wahidah) also has two possibilities: first, to consider 'wahidah' as an adjective of an omitted infinitive, i.e., 'one divorce'; second, that the husband is praising her, saying she is unique in his time or among his people. If he intends the first meaning, it is as if he said 'Anti tathliqatun wahidatun', and a revocable divorce occurs. Summary: Since these three words can imply both divorce and non-divorce, intention is necessary for divorce. And only one revocable divorce occurs. The proof is that in the first two words, 'Anti taliq' is established by implication, and in the third word, 'talaq' is implied. If his words 'Anti taliq' or 'tathliq' were explicit, only one would occur, so when it is implied, it is even more so. And regarding his word 'wahidah', there is an answer to an objection. The objection is that in 'Anti wahidah', the infinitive talaq is mentioned, so intending three should be valid. The answer: Specifying 'one' is contrary to intending three. For this reason, intending three with 'wahidah' is not valid. The author of Hidayah says: The i'rab of 'wahidah' in 'Anti wahidah' is not considered. Whether it is read with nasb, raf', or sukun, in all three cases divorce occurs. This is the view of the general mashayikh and it is correct. Some mashayikh hold that if it is with nasb, divorce occurs even without intention; if with raf', divorce does not occur even with intention; if with sukun, intention is needed. The first is correct because common people cannot distinguish between forms of i'rab. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Al-Hidayah commentary. It defines the first type of kinayah talaq: three specific words that produce only one revocable (raj'i) divorce. The words are: (1) I'taddi (count the 'iddah), (2) Istabri'i rahimaki (purify your womb), and (3) Anti wahidah (you are one). Each word has two possible meanings: one related to divorce, the other unrelated. For I'taddi: it can mean counting the 'iddah (divorce-related) or counting Allah's blessings (non-divorce). For Istabri'i rahimaki: it can mean completing the 'iddah (divorce-related) or observing istibra' to give a sunnah divorce (non-divorce directly). For Anti wahidah: it can be an adjective of an omitted infinitive meaning 'one divorce' (divorce-related) or praising the wife as unique (non-divorce). Because these words are ambiguous, intention (niyyah) is required to make it a divorce. The ruling is that only one revocable divorce occurs, even if three intend, because the implicit 'Anti taliq' or similar can only produce one. The passage also addresses an objection regarding 'Anti wahidah': since 'talaq' is mentioned in the implied form, why cannot three be intended? The response is that specifying 'wahidah' (one) negates the intention of three. Additionally, the i'rab (case endings) of 'wahidah' does not affect the ruling according to the majority of Hanafi scholars (general mashayikh), because common people cannot distinguish. This is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kinayah): An allusive or indirect expression that can mean divorce or other things, requiring intention to specify.
رجعی (raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new contract.
نیة (niyyah): Intention in the heart to effect a divorce when using a kinayah word.
واحدة (wahidah): The word 'one' used in the phrase 'Anti wahidah', indicating a single divorce unit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'I'taddi' (count the iddah) with the intention of divorce. How many divorces occur and what type?
A) One revocable divorce
B) One irrevocable divorce
C) Three divorces if intended
D) No divorce because the word is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
When a husband uses one of the three kinayah words (I'taddi, Istabri'i rahimaki, Anti wahidah) with intention of divorce, only one revocable divorce occurs, regardless of i'rab.

Remaining Kinayah Words: One Irrevocable or Three
Listing the remaining kinayah words and their ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for the remaining kinayah words and apply the rule of intention to determine the number of divorces.

Core Idea
For non-special kinayah words, intention alone determines one ba'in or three divorces.

In the previous lesson, we learned that three specific kinayah words produce only one revocable divorce. Now, we turn to the remaining twenty-two kinayah phrases. These words are more flexible: they can yield one irrevocable divorce or three divorces, depending entirely on the husband's intention. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying divorce rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the class
Recognize that the passage lists all kinayah words except the three special ones (I'taddi, Istabri'i, Anti wahidah). These are the 'remaining' kinayah.
The three special words are covered in a previous lesson.
2
State the default ruling
For these remaining words, if the husband intends divorce, the result is one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce.
This is the default when one divorce is intended.
3
Apply the intention variable
If he intends three, three divorces occur; if he intends two, it is treated as one ba'in (since two is not a recognized number in this context).
Intention determines the count, not the wording.
4
Explain the underlying principle
These words are ambiguous; they can imply divorce or other meanings (e.g., permission, separation), so intention (niyyah) is necessary. Without intention, no divorce occurs.
This contrasts with the three special words which have an implicit link to divorce.

بقیہ کنایات سے طلاق کی نیت ہو تو ایک طلاق بائن اور اگر تین کی نسبت ہو تو تین طلاقیں واقع ہوتی ہیں
قال و بقية الكنايات اذانوى بها الطلاق كانت واحدة بائنة وان نوى ثلثا كان ثلثا وان نوى ثنتين كانت واحدة بائنة و هذا مثل قوله انت بائن وبتة وبتلة و حرام و حبلک علی غاربک والحقی باهلک و وخلية وبرية ووهبتك لاهلک و سرحتک و فارقتک و امرک بیدک و اختاری وانت حرة وتقنعى وتخمرى و استتری و اغربی و اخرجي واذهبي وقومي و ابتغى الازواج لانها تحتمل الطلاق وغيره فلابد من النية
From the remaining kinayah, if divorce is intended, it is one irrevocable divorce; if three are intended, three divorces occur. He said: And the remaining kinayah: if he intends divorce with them, it is one irrevocable divorce; if he intends three, it is three; if he intends two, it is one irrevocable. This is like his words: 'Anti ba'in', 'battah', 'battalah', 'haram', 'habluki 'ala gharibiki', 'ilhaqi bi-ahliki', 'khaliah', 'bariyah', 'wahabtuki li-ahliki', 'sarrahtuki', 'faraqtuki', 'amruki bi-yadiki', 'ikhtari', 'anti hurrah', 'taqanna'i', 'takhammari', 'istatri', 'ughrubi', 'ukhruji', 'idhhabi', 'qumi', 'ibtaghi al-azwaj'. Because they can imply divorce and other things, so intention is necessary.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for all kinayah words other than the three mentioned earlier. It is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Hidayah. The rule is: for all remaining kinayah words (a list of 22 words/phrases is given), if the husband intends one divorce, one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce occurs; if he intends three, three divorces occur; if he intends two, it is treated as one irrevocable (since two is not a recognized count in kinayah - it becomes one ba'in). The list includes words like 'Anti ba'in' (you are separated), 'battah' (absolutely), 'haram' (forbidden), etc. The underlying principle is that these words are ambiguous between divorce and other meanings (e.g., praise, separation), so intention (niyyah) is required. Unlike the previous three words which are restricted to one revocable, these can produce either one ba'in or three, depending on intention. The key distinction: the earlier three words are considered to contain an implicit 'Anti taliq' which limits them to one revocable; these remaining words do not have that implicit connection, so they can produce multiple divorces if intended.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new contract.
بتة (battah): A kinayah word meaning 'absolutely' or 'finally', used for divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Three divorces

Key Takeaway
For all remaining kinayah words, intention is decisive: one intended gives one ba'in, three gives three, and two is treated as one ba'in.

Talaq — Divorce

Ruling on Three Specific Kinayah Words for Divorce
Explaining the three kinayah words and the need for intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three kinayah words for divorce, explain why intention (niyyah) is required, and apply the mufta bihi ruling regarding the grammatical case of 'واحدة'.

Core Idea
Kinayah words require intention; only one revocable divorce occurs regardless of grammatical case.

In this passage, Al-Marghinani examines three allusive expressions that a husband might use to divorce his wife: 'اعتدی', 'استبری رحمك', and 'أنت واحدة'. Each of these can convey a divorce meaning or a non-divorce meaning, so the husband's intention becomes decisive. The passage also addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the grammatical case of 'واحدة' affects the ruling, settling on the majority view that it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three kinayah words and their dual meanings
List the three expressions and recognise that each can imply divorce or something else (e.g., praise, instruction). Understand why they are considered kinayah (allusive) rather than sarih (explicit).
The dual meaning is the key reason intention is required.
2
Determine the role of intention (niyyah)
Since the words are ambiguous, the husband's intention specifies the intended meaning. If he intends divorce, only one revocable divorce occurs; without intention, no divorce occurs.
Intention is wajib (obligatory) for divorce to take effect with these words.
3
Analyse the proof (dalil) for one revocable divorce
The proof is based on analogy: if explicit words like 'أنت طالق' produce only one divorce, then implied words (via kinayah) produce one a fortiori (أولى). This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
The proof is given in the text: 'إذا ظهرت تطليقة واحدة فالمقدر أولى'.
4
Address the objection regarding intending three divorces with 'واحدة'
An opponent might argue that since 'واحدة' can be an adjective of an implied 'تطليقة', one could intend three. The refutation: the specification of 'واحدة' (one) contradicts the intention of three.
This objection is answered in the text.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf on grammatical case of 'واحدة' and identify mufta bihi
Some early scholars held that accusative (نصب) triggers divorce without intention, nominative (رفع) blocks it even with intention, and sukoon requires intention. The majority, including the author, holds that case is irrelevant; divorce occurs if intended, regardless of case. This is the mufta bihi.
The reason: laypeople cannot distinguish cases correctly.

Source Text

Count. Then if the husband intended the first meaning, his intention specifies the first meaning. And that meaning requires that divorce precede, because the command to observe the waiting period is not valid without divorce. Therefore, it is necessary to consider the utterance as having preceded. And the second word (استبرائی رحمک) also has two probabilities: one is that she should observe the waiting period, because the purpose of passing the waiting period is to purify the womb, so it is as if the waiting period was explicitly mentioned. So this phrase استبری رحمک is on the level of اعتدی. The other meaning is to purify the womb from menstruation so that divorce can be given in the sunnah manner. In this case too, if he intended the meaning, his intention specifies the first meaning. And that meaning necessitates that divorce preceded. As for the third word انت واحدة, it also has two meanings: first, that واحدة is an adjective of a suppressed infinitive, i.e., تطلیقہ واحدۃ (a single divorce). The second meaning is that the husband praises her, saying she is unique in his time or in his tribe, meaning there is no other woman like her. In this case too, if he intended the first meaning, it is as if he said انت تطلیقۃ واحدۃ, and from this speech a revocable divorce occurs. Therefore, from انت واحدة also one revocable divorce occurs. Summary: When these three words have both a divorce and non-divorce meaning, intention is necessary to take the meaning of divorce. And from these words one revocable divorce occurs. The proof is that in the first two words, اعتدی and استبری رحمک, the implication of انت طالق (you are divorced) is established. And in the third word, انت واحدة, the word divorce is implied (مقدر). If he had said explicitly انت طالق or تطلیقہ, only one divorce would occur. So when the word divorce is implied, all the more reason that one occurs. And in قوله واحدة there is an answer to an objection: The objection is that in انت واحدة the infinitive of divorce is mentioned, so intending three divorces should be correct. The answer is that the specification of واحدة contradicts the intention of three. For this reason, intending three with واحدة is not correct. The author of Hidayah says: In انت واحدة, the grammatical case of واحدة is not considered. Whether it is read in the accusative, nominative, or with sukoon, in all three cases divorce occurs. This is the view of the majority of scholars and it is correct. Some scholars hold that if واحدة is in the accusative, divorce occurs even if he did not intend divorce; if in the nominative, divorce does not occur even if he intended divorce; if read with sukoon, it requires intention. The first is correct because common people are unable to distinguish between grammatical cases. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding three specific kinayah (allusive) words for divorce: اعتدی (observe the waiting period), استبری رحمک (purify your womb), and انت واحدة (you are one). The commentator explains that each of these words can be interpreted in two ways: either as a command or statement implying divorce, or as something else unrelated to divorce (e.g., praise or instruction). Since they are ambiguous, the husband's intention (niyyah) is necessary to determine which meaning is intended. If the husband intends the divorce meaning, only one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) occurs, because the implication or supposed word of divorce is singular. The proof offered is that if explicit words like 'you are divorced' would give only one divorce, then implied words give one a fortiori. The passage also answers an objection: one might think that since the word 'واحدة' can be an adjective for an implied 'divorce', one could intend three divorces. But the specification of 'واحدة' (one) prevents that. Additionally, the grammatical case (i'rab) of 'واحدة' does not affect the ruling; divorce occurs regardless of whether it is read with nasb, raf', or sukoon, because common people are not experts in grammar. This is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) according to the author of Hidayah and the majority of mashayikh. Some earlier scholars had a different view based on grammatical cases, but the correct view is the former.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kināyah): An indirect or allusive expression that may or may not refer to divorce; requires intention to effect divorce.
نیت (niyyah): Intention of the husband to divorce when using a kinayah word; essential for divorce to occur.
اعتدی (i‘taddī): A command to the wife to observe the waiting period ('iddah); one of the three kinayah words for divorce discussed.
استبری رحمک (istabri raḥimak): A phrase meaning 'purify your womb'; one of the three kinayah words.
أنت واحدة (anti wāḥidah): Literally 'you are one'; a kinayah expression for divorce or praise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It always effects one revocable divorce regardless of intention.
B) It effects one revocable divorce only if the husband intends divorce.
C) It effects three divorces if the husband intends three.

Key Takeaway
Three specific kinayah words require intention for divorce; only one revocable divorce results, and grammatical case does not affect the ruling.

Ruling on Remaining Kinayah Words for Divorce
Listing remaining kinayah words and their ruling with intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for each of the remaining kinayah words and apply the intention rule to determine the number of divorces.

Core Idea
For kinayah words, intention decides the count: one or two intended yields one ba'in; three intended yields three.

In this passage, the author continues from the previous lesson and lists numerous allusive words (kinayah) beyond the initial three. The general rule is established: intention is necessary, and the number of divorces depends on what the husband intended. Notably, these kinayah words produce an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) even if only one is intended. This differs from the three specific words that produce a revocable divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category and rule
Recognize that these are the 'remaining kinayah' words, distinct from the three specific kinayah discussed earlier. The rule for all: if the husband intends divorce, it takes effect; intention is required.
The rule is summarized at the beginning: one or two intended = one ba'in; three intended = three ba'in.
2
Memorize the list of words
Work through the list phrase by phrase. Notice they include statements like 'you are ba'in', 'you are haram', and commands like 'go away', 'stand', 'seek husbands'.
All these words can also mean non-divorce things. Focus on the divorce meaning.
3
Apply the intention rule
Given a scenario, determine the outcome: if the husband says one of these words with a clear intention (e.g., three), the divorce count matches that intention. If intention is absent, no divorce occurs.
The default is no divorce if intention is unknown.

And if he intends two, then it is one ba'in. And these are like his words: 'you are ba'in', 'bat', 'batla', 'haram', 'your rope is on your shoulders', 'join your family', 'khaliyah', 'bariyah', 'I gifted you to your family', 'I released you', 'I separated from you', 'your matter is in your hand', 'choose', 'you are free', 'cover your face', 'cover with khimar', 'veil yourself', 'go away', 'leave', 'go', 'stand', 'seek husbands'. Explanation: Here the author, may Allah have mercy on him, is explaining the second category of kinayah, i.e., besides the mentioned three words, the remaining kinayah words produce one ba'in divorce if one is intended or two, and if three are intended then three occur. All the kinayah words mentioned in the text have both a divorce and non-divorce meaning. For this reason, intention is necessary to specify the meaning of divorce. And it is not difficult to extract two meanings from each word. For example, the last word 'wa ibtaghi al-azwaj' has one meaning: seek out women who are your peers so that your heart may (be content), and the other meaning: seek husbands because you have become ba'in from me. And so on. And Allah knows best. Jameel, may He forgive him.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing the remaining kinayah (allusive) words for divorce beyond the three previously discussed. The author states the general rule: if the husband uses any of these words and intends divorce, the result is one irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) if he intended one or two, and three divorces if he intended three. This is because these words are ambiguous; they can mean divorce or something else. The list includes phrases like 'you are ba'in', 'you are haram', 'your rope is on your shoulder', 'join your family', 'I gifted you to your family', 'choose', 'you are free', commands like 'cover your face', 'go away', 'leave', 'stand', and 'seek husbands'. All of these are kinayah, meaning they are not explicitly about divorce but can be interpreted as such. The commentator clarifies that this is the second category; the first category was the three specific words (اعتدی, etc.) which produce a revocable divorce. For these remaining words, the divorce is ba'in (irrevocable) even if only one is intended. The reason for the difference is that these words are more remote from explicit divorce and usually imply final separation. The passage also explains by example: 'seek husbands' (ابتغی الازواج) can mean she should look for a husband because she is divorced, or it can mean she should find women to marry (a different context). Thus intention is critical.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (talāq bā'in): An irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage and her consent.
کنایہ (kināyah): An expression that is not explicit for divorce but can be interpreted as such; requires intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The listed kinayah words require intention; intending one or two yields one ba'in, intending three yields three ba'in.

Three States and Three Types of Kinayah in Talaq
Ruling with contextual proof for kinayah in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify kinayah words into three types and determine whether divorce occurs in qada' based on the husband's state and the type of kinayah.

Core Idea
Context matters: in discussing divorce, certain kinayah words effect divorce without intention in court.

This passage delves into the Hanafi approach to kinayah (allusive) words in divorce. It establishes that the effect of kinayah is not uniform; it depends on two variables: the state of the husband at the time of utterance (pleasure, discussing divorce, or anger) and the linguistic nature of the word (whether it can serve as a reply, rejection, or insult). Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying the ruling in judicial practice and for distinguishing between what happens in court (qada') and what is valid between the husband and Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three states
List the three states mentioned: state of pleasure (hāl al-riḍā), state of discussing divorce (hāl mudhākarat al-ṭalāq), and state of anger (hāl al-ghaḍab). Each has a different presumption regarding the husband's intention.
The state of pleasure is the default; the other two are exceptional and create a contextual indication (qarīna).
2
Classify kinayah into three types
Categorize kinayah words: (1) words that can be a reply (jawāb) and a rejection (radd), (2) words that can be a reply but not a rejection, (3) words that can be a reply and also an insult (sabb). This classification is based on the word's potential usage in normal conversation.
Examples: 'idhhabi' (go) can be a reply and rejection; 'khaliyah' (free) is a reply but not rejection; 'i'taddi' (prepare) can be a reply and insult.
3
Apply the ruling for the state of pleasure
In the state of pleasure, none of the kinayah words effects divorce without the husband's intention (niyya). The husband's denial of intention is accepted in court because there is no external indication of divorce.
This is the default rule: kinayah requires intention. The burden of proof is on the wife if she claims divorce.
4
Apply the ruling for the state of discussing divorce
When divorce is being discussed, for words that are only a reply and not a rejection (e.g., 'ba'in', 'khaliyah'), divorce occurs in court (qada') even if the husband denies intention. The context indicates he intends divorce. For words that are also rejections, his denial is accepted because the word could be just a dismissal.
The key distinction: if the word can be a normal rejection (like 'go'), the husband is believed; if it cannot be a rejection (like 'severed'), the apparent meaning is divorce.
5
Apply the ruling for the state of anger
In anger, the husband is believed for all kinayah words except those that are only suitable for divorce and not for rejection or insult (e.g., 'i'taddi', 'ikhtari', 'amruki bi-yadiki'). Anger indicates a desire to divorce, so in those words the denial is not accepted. Abu Yusuf adds that phrases like 'la mulka li alayki' are believed in anger because they can also be insults.
Abu Yusuf's view is an intra-Hanafi nuance: some words in anger are ambiguous between divorce and insult, so the husband is given the benefit of the doubt.

قال الا ان يكون فى حالة مذاكرة الطلاق فيقع بها الطلاق فى القضاء ولا يقع فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى الا ان ينويه قال سوى بين هذه الالفاظ وهذا فيما لا يصلح ردا والجملة في ذلك ان الاحوال ثلثة حالة مطلقة و هي حالة الرضاء وحالة مذاكرة الطلاق وحالة الغضب والكنايات ثلثة اقسام ما يصلح جوابا وردا وما يصلح جوابا لارداو ما يصلح جوابا ويصلح سبا وشتيمة ففى حالة الرضاء لا يكون شيء منها طلاقا الا بالنية والقول قوله فى انكار النية لماقلنا وفى حالة مذاكرة الطلاق لم يصدق فيما يصلح جوابا ولا يصلح ردافى القضاء مثل قوله خلية وبرية بائن بتة حرام اعتدى امرك بیدک اختاری لان الظاهران مراده الطلاق عندسوال الطلاق يصدق فيما يصلح جوابا وردامثل قوله اذهبي اخرجی قومی تقنعی تخمرى ومايجرى هذا المجرى لانه يحتمل الردوهو الادنى فحمل عليه وفى حالة الغضب يصدق فى جميع ذلك لاحتمال الرد والسب الافيما يصلح للطلاق ولا يصلح للرد والشتم كقوله اعتدی و اختاری و امرک بیدک فانه لا يصدق فيها لان الغضب يدل على ارادة الطلاق وعن ابى یوسف فى قوله لا ملک لی علیک و لاسبیل لی علیک و خلیت سبیلک و فارقتك انه يصدق فى حالة الغضب لمافيها من احتمال معنى السب ترجمہ کہا مگر یہ کہ مذاکرہ طلاق کی حالت میں ہو تو ان الفاظ سے طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی قضاء اور فیما بینہ و بین اللہ واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ مگر یہ کہ طلاق کی نیت کرے۔ مصنف نے فرمایا کہ (قدوری) نے ان الفاظ کو برابر کر دیا۔ حالانکہ یہ حکم جو قدوری نے بیان فرمایا ہے ) ان الفاظ میں ہے جو رد کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتے ہیں۔ اس بارے میں حاصل یہ ہے کہ حالتیں تین ہیں: (1) مطلقہ اور یہ رضامندی کی حالت ہے۔ طلاق کے ذکر واذکار کی حالت ۔
He said: Except if it is in a state of discussing divorce, then divorce occurs by them in the court (qada') but does not occur between him and Allah unless he intends it. He said: He (Quduri) made these words equal. And this ruling applies to those words that cannot serve as a reply. The summary is that the states are three: the absolute state, which is the state of pleasure; the state of discussing divorce; and the state of anger. And the kinayah are three types: those that can serve as a reply and rejection; those that can serve as a reply but not rejection; and those that can serve as a reply and also as insult and abuse. So in the state of pleasure, none of them is divorce except with intention, and his claim in denying intention is accepted, as we said. In the state of discussing divorce, he is not believed regarding words that can serve as a reply but not rejection, in the court, like his words: khaliyah, bariyah, ba'in, bat, haram, i'taddi, amruki bi-yadiki, ikhtari, because the apparent meaning is that he intends divorce when divorce is being discussed. He is believed regarding words that can serve as a reply and rejection, like his words: idhhabi, ukhruji, qumi, taqanna'i, takhammari, and what proceeds in this manner, because it can be a rejection, which is the lower meaning, so it is interpreted accordingly. In the state of anger, he is believed in all of that due to the possibility of rejection and abuse, except for words that are suitable for divorce but not for rejection and abuse, like his words: i'taddi, ikhtari, amruki bi-yadiki, for he is not believed in them because anger indicates the intention of divorce. And from Abu Yusuf, regarding his words 'I have no ownership over you', 'I have no way over you', 'I have released your path', 'I have separated from you', that he is believed in the state of anger because they contain the possibility of abusive meaning. Translation: He said: Except if it is in the state of discussing divorce, then divorce occurs by these words in the court but does not occur between him and Allah unless he intends it. The author said that (Quduri) made these words equal. Although this ruling that Quduri mentioned applies to words that do not have the capacity for rejection. The gist of the matter is that the states are three: (1) the absolute state, which is the state of pleasure; the state of discussing divorce.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that introduces a crucial contextual distinction for kinayah words. The author explains that the effect of kinayah words depends on three states: (1) state of pleasure (رضا), (2) state of discussing divorce (مذاكرة الطلاق), and (3) state of anger (غضب). Additionally, kinayah words are classified into three types: those that can be used as both a reply (جواب) and a rejection (رد), those that can be a reply but not a rejection, and those that can be a reply and also an insult (سب). In the state of pleasure, no divorce occurs without intention, and the husband's denial of intention is accepted. In the state of discussing divorce, for words that can be a reply but not a rejection (e.g., خلية, برية, بائن, etc.), divorce occurs in court (قضاء) even if he denies intention, because the context suggests he intends divorce. But for words that can be a reply and rejection (e.g., اذهبي, اخرجي, etc.), his denial is accepted because the word could be a rejection, which is the lesser meaning. In the state of anger, his denial is accepted for all words except those that are only suitable for divorce and not for rejection or insult (e.g., اعتدی, اختاری, امرک بیدک), because anger itself indicates his desire to divorce. Finally, a transmission from Abu Yusuf states that phrases like 'I have no ownership over you' etc., are believed in anger because they could be insults. This nuanced treatment shows the Hanafi method of considering both the linguistic nature of the words and the surrounding circumstances.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍā’): In judicial context; a divorce that occurs in the court's ruling based on apparent circumstances, regardless of the husband's inner intention.
رضا (riḍā): State of pleasure or normal conditions; no external pressure or indication of divorce.
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): Context where the topic of divorce is being discussed between the couple or among others.
غضب (ghaḍab): State of anger; the husband is visibly angry, which indicates a possible desire to divorce but also possibility of insult.
جواب (jawāb): A reply or response in conversation; kinayah words that can be used as a reply.
رد (radd): Rejection or dismissal; a word that can be used to reject or dismiss the wife.
سب (sabb): Insult or abuse; a word that could be intended as an insult rather than divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which state is the husband believed (his denial of intention accepted) for words that are only a reply and not a rejection (e.g., 'ba'in')?
A) State of pleasure only
B) State of discussing divorce only
C) State of anger only
D) Never believed for such words

Key Takeaway
Divorce by kinayah depends on the husband's state and the word's linguistic capacity; in discussing divorce, words that are only replies effect divorce without intention in court.

Kinayat Phrases in Talaq: The Role of Intention
Listing kinayat phrases and their ruling with niyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kinayat talaq and apply the requirement of niyyah to determine the number of divorces.

Core Idea
Kinayat phrases require niyyah — without intention, no divorce; with intention, one bain divorce for one or two intentions, three for three.

This lesson covers the second category of kinayat phrases in Hanafi divorce law. The author lists several expressions that carry dual meaning. The key principle: the husband's intention determines whether a divorce occurs and how many. Understanding this is essential for correctly applying talaq rulings in ambiguous cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of expression
Determine if the words are kinayat (ambiguous) or sarih (clear). Kinayat require niyyah.
Sarih words like 'talaq' do not need niyyah.
2
Determine the husband's intention
Ask: did he intend divorce? If yes, how many? One, two, or three?
Intention for two results in one bain divorce.
3
Apply the ruling
If he intended one or two divorces, one bain divorce occurs. If three, three divorces occur. No divorce if no intention.
Bain divorce is irrevocable.

Translation: And the remaining kinayat: when divorce is intended by them, one bain divorce occurs. And if three are intended, three occur. And if two are intended, one bain divorce occurs. And these are, for example: his saying 'you are bain', 'battah', 'battalah', 'cut off', 'haram', 'your affair is on your neck', 'join your family', 'you are torn', 'you are free', 'I gifted you to your family', 'I left you', 'I separated from you', 'your affair is in your hand', 'choose', 'you are free', 'cover your face with a niqab', 'wear a headscarf', 'cover yourself', 'go away', 'leave', 'walk', 'stand up', and 'seek a spouse'. Because these words carry the possibility of both divorce and non-divorce, thus niyyah is necessary. Explanation: Here the author is explaining the second category of kinayat. That is, from the kinayat other than the three words mentioned, one bain divorce occurs if one is intended, and if two are intended, one bain, and if three are intended, three occur. All the kinayat words mentioned in the text have the possibility of both divorce and non-divorce. For this reason niyyah is necessary to specify the meaning of divorce. And extracting two meanings from each word is not difficult. For example, the last word 'tabtaghi al-azwaj' has one meaning: 'seek women of your kind (i.e., a wife)' and another meaning: 'seek husbands because you have become bain from me'. And so on. Allah knows best. Jamil, forgive him.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling statement regarding the second category of kinayat (metaphorical expressions) for divorce. The author lists several Arabic/Urdu phrases such as 'you are bain', 'you are battah', 'you are haram', 'your affair is in your hand', etc. He states that if the husband intends divorce by these words, one bain divorce occurs if he intends one, and three occur if he intends three. If he intends two, it also results in one bain divorce because two is not a specific number for talaq. The key principle is that these words have dual potential: they could mean divorce or something else (like a command to leave or a statement of separation). Therefore, the husband's intention (niyyah) is necessary to determine the actual talaq. If no intention, no divorce occurs. The author also gives an example: 'tabtaghi al-azwaj' could mean 'seek a spouse' (i.e., remarry) or 'seek new husbands (for yourself)' implying divorce. This is from the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kināyah): An expression that can imply divorce or something else, requiring niyyah to determine the intended meaning.
بائنہ (bā'inah): A type of divorce that is irrevocable, meaning the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage.
نیت (niyyah): Intention in the heart to effect divorce; required for kinayat to specify that divorce is meant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'you are free' (a kinayah) and intends two divorces, what is the ruling?
A) Two bain divorces occur
B) One bain divorce occurs
C) Three divorces occur
D) No divorce occurs because the word is not clear

Key Takeaway
For kinayat talaq, the husband's intention is decisive: without niyyah no divorce, with intention for one or two yields one bain, with intention for three yields three.

Three States and Three Types of Kinayat
Classifying states and types of kinayat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the three states and three types of kinayat and apply the Hanafi rule for when niyyah is necessary for divorce to occur in court.

Core Idea
Divorce by kinayah depends on the state and the type of word; context determines intention.

Divorce through indirect words (kinayah) is not straightforward. The Hanafi school considers the surrounding circumstances and the nature of the word itself. This lesson unpacks the key classifications from the Hidayah: three states of the husband and three categories of kinayat, which together determine whether the husband's denial of intention is accepted in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three states
List the states: state of contentment (rida), state of discussing divorce (mudhakara), and state of anger (ghadab). Each state alters the ruling on when divorce occurs without intention.
Mudhakara includes when the wife or a third party asks for divorce.
2
Classify the three types of kinayat
Determine whether the kinayah word is (1) valid as both a response and a rejection, (2) valid as a response but not a rejection, or (3) valid as a response and also as abuse. Examples: 'idhhabi' (go) is type 1; 'i'tiddi' (observe iddah) is type 2; 'la milka li alayki' (I have no power over you) is type 3.
Type 1 words have two possible meanings; the lesser (rejection) is given priority.
3
Match state with type to find the ruling
For each state, apply the rule: in rida, no divorce without intention for any type; in mudhakara, for type 2 words divorce occurs without intention in court, but for type 1 words the husband is believed if he claims rejection; in ghadab, the husband is believed for all except type 2 words that cannot be abuse.
The exception in ghadab is for words solely for divorce, like 'i'tiddi'.
4
Analyze the Abu Yusuf ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that in the state of anger, the husband is believed even for words like 'la milka li alayki' because they can be interpreted as abuse, unlike the majority view that such words are still believed (but for different reasons).
This affects only words that could be both divorce and abuse.

کنایات میں تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ رد کا یا گالی کا احتمال رکھنے کی وجہ سے ۔ مگر ان الفاظ میں جورد اور گالی کی صلاحیت نہ رکھتے ہوں۔ اور طلاق میں تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ غصہ ، طلاق مراد لینے پر دلالت کرتا ہے۔ اور ابو یوسف سے روایت ہے کہ اس کے قول میں کہ میری تجھ پر کوئی ملک نہیں۔ اور نہ میری تجھ پر کوئی راہ ہے۔ اور میں نے تیری راہ چھوڑ دی اور میں نے تجھ سے تجھ کو جدا کر دیا غصہ کی حالت میں تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ ان صورتوں میں گالی کے معنی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ تشریح سابقہ مسئلہ میں گذر چکا کہ لفظ کنایہ کے ذریعہ بلانیت طلاق طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ مگر یہ کہ مذاکرۃ طلاق کی حالت میں ہو۔ قدوری کی اس عبارت کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ لفظ کنایہ کے ساتھ بلانیت طلاق ، طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ ہاں اگر میاں بیوی کے درمیان طلاق کا مذاکرہ ہورہا ہے اور اسی حالت میں شوہر نے کسی لفظ کنایہ سے عورت کو مخاطب کیا تو اس حالت میں وقوع طلاق کیلئے نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ بلانیت طلاق قضاء طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اگر چہ دیا نہ فیما بنیہ و بین اللہ بلانیت کے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قدوری نے مذاکرہ طلاق کی حالت میں بلانیت وقوع طلاق کے بارے میں تمام الفاظ کنایہ کو برابر کر دیا۔ حالانکہ یہ حکم مطلق نہیں ۔ بلکہ صرف ان الفاظ میں ہے جورد کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتے ہیں۔ چنانچہ اس کی تفصیل یہ ہے کہ احوال تین ہیں: (1) حالت مطلقہ یعنی رضا مندی کی حالت (۲) مذاکرہ طلاق کی حالت مثلاً عورت اپنے شوہر سے طلاق کا سوال کر رہی ہے یا کوئی دوسرا آدمی اس کی طلاق کا اس کے شوہر سے سوال کر رہا ہے۔ (۳) حالت غضب یعنی دونوں طرف سے غصہ کی باتیں ہو رہی ہیں۔ یعنی سے غصہ باتیں ہورہی اسی طرح کنایات کی بھی تین قسمیں ہیں: وہ الفاظ کنایہ جو جواب اور دونوں ہو سکتے ہیں۔ یعنی عورت کی جانب سے جو طلاق کا سوال کیا گیا ہے۔ اس کا جواب بھی ہو سکتے ہیں ۔ اور ا سکے کلام کار د بھی۔
Regarding the mentioned divorce, divorce occurs without intention. He said: Except if it is in the state of discussing divorce, then divorce occurs by it in the qada (court), but does not occur between him and Allah unless he intends it. He said: He equated these words, and this is for those words which are not valid as rejection. The summary is that there are three states: the absolute state (state of contentment), the state of discussing divorce, and the state of anger. And kinayat are of three types: what is valid as both response and rejection, what is valid as response but not rejection, and what is valid as response and also as abuse. In the state of contentment, none of them is divorce except with intention, and the husband's word is accepted in denying intention due to what we said. In the state of discussing divorce, he is not believed (in court) in words that are valid as response but not rejection, such as his saying 'khaliyah', 'bariyah', 'bain', 'battah', 'haram', 'i'tiddi', 'amruki biyadik', 'ikhtari', because the apparent is that he intends divorce when divorce is asked. He is believed in words that are valid as both response and rejection, such as his saying 'idhhabi', 'ukhruji', 'qumi', 'taqanna'i', 'takhammari' and similar, because they carry the possibility of rejection, and rejection is the lesser meaning, so it is given priority. In the state of anger, he is believed in all of that due to the possibility of rejection and abuse, except in words that are valid for divorce and not valid for rejection or abuse, such as 'i'tiddi', 'ikhtari', 'amruki biyadik', for he is not believed in them because anger indicates the intention of divorce. And from Abu Yusuf, regarding his saying 'I have no ownership over you', 'I have no way over you', 'I have freed your way', 'I have parted from you', he is believed in the state of anger because there is the possibility of the meaning of abuse. Translation: He said: Except if it is in the state of discussing divorce, then divorce occurs by these words in the qada, but does not occur between him and Allah unless he intends it. The author said: Al-Quduri equated these words, but this ruling applies only to those words that are not valid as rejection. The summary is that there are three states: (1) The absolute state, which is the state of contentment, (2) the state of discussing divorce, (3) the state of anger. And kinayat are also of three types: (1) what is valid as both response and rejection, (2) what is valid as response but not rejection, (3) what is valid as response and also as abuse. So in the state of contentment, no divorce occurs from these words except with intention, and the husband's word is accepted in denying intention. In the state of discussing divorce, he is not believed in court in words that are valid as response but not rejection, e.g., 'khaliyah', 'bariyah', 'bain', 'battah', 'haram', 'i'tiddi', 'amruki biyadik', 'ikhtari', because the apparent is that he intends divorce when divorce is asked. He is believed in words that are valid as both response and rejection, e.g., 'idhhabi', 'ukhruji', 'qumi', 'taqanna'i', 'takhammari' and similar, because they carry the possibility of rejection, and rejection is the lesser meaning, so it is given priority. In the state of anger, he is believed in all of them due to the possibility of rejection and abuse, except in words that are not valid for rejection or abuse but are valid for divorce, e.g., 'i'tiddi', 'ikhtari', 'amruki biyadik', for he is not believed because anger indicates the intention of divorce. And it is reported from Abu Yusuf that in his saying 'I have no ownership over you', 'I have no way over you', 'I have freed your way', 'I have parted from you', he is believed in the state of anger because there is the possibility of the meaning of abuse. Explanation: In the previous issue it was mentioned that divorce does not occur without intention through a kinayah word, except in the state of discussing divorce. The summary of Quduri's statement is that divorce does not occur without intention with a kinayah word. Yes, if the husband and wife are discussing divorce and in that state the husband addresses the wife with some kinayah, then in this state intention is not necessary for the occurrence of divorce. Rather, without intention divorce occurs in court, although between him and Allah divorce does not occur without intention. The author of Hidayah says that Quduri equated all kinayah words in the state of discussing divorce regarding the occurrence of divorce without intention. But this ruling is not absolute; rather, it applies only to those words that are not valid as rejection. Thus, the detail is that there are three states: (1) The absolute state, i.e., state of contentment, (2) the state of discussing divorce, e.g., the woman is asking her husband for divorce or another person is asking him, (3) the state of anger, where angry words are exchanged. Similarly, kinayat are of three types: those that can be both a response and a rejection; i.e., they can be a response to the woman's request for divorce and also a rejection of her request.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling about kinayat by introducing three states (ahwal) and three types of kinayat. The states are: (1) state of contentment (rida), where the husband is calm; (2) state of discussing divorce (mudhakara), where divorce is being talked about; (3) state of anger (ghadab). The types of kinayat are: (1) words suitable as both a response to a divorce request and a rejection (i.e., they can be interpreted as not granting divorce); (2) words suitable as a response but not as a rejection; (3) words suitable as a response and also as abuse. The ruling varies by state and type. In the state of contentment, no divorce occurs without intention (niyyah). In the state of discussing divorce, for type 2 words (response only), divorce occurs without intention in court (qada) and the husband is not believed if he claims he did not intend divorce, because the context shows he intended to grant divorce. For type 1 words (response and rejection), the husband is believed if he says he meant rejection, because rejection is the lesser meaning. In the state of anger, the husband is believed for all words except for those that are only suitable for divorce (not for rejection or abuse) because anger indicates an intention to divorce. Abu Yusuf adds that even for words like 'I have no ownership over you', the husband is believed in anger since they could be construed as abuse. The Hanafi position is that the mufta bihi is the detailed distinction provided in the Hidayah, not the blanket ruling of Quduri.

Key Terms
مذاکرہ طلاق (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): The state where divorce is being discussed between spouses or with a third party asking for divorce.
حالت غضب (ḥālat ghaḍab): The state of anger where abusive language may be exchanged; affects credibility of the husband's denial of intent.
رد (radd): Rejection; in this context, kinayat that can be interpreted as rejecting the woman's request for divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the state of mudhakarat al-talaq (discussing divorce), if the husband says 'idhhabi' (go away) to his wife, and later claims he did not intend divorce, is he believed in court?
A) Yes, because the word 'idhhabi' can be a rejection of her request, so his claim is accepted.
B) No, because in this state he is never believed for any kinayah.
C) Yes, because he is always believed in denying intention.
D) No, because 'idhhabi' is only suitable as a response, not as rejection.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of states and types of kinayat is essential for determining when divorce occurs without intention in court.
